    learning_rate=0.1,     # step size
    subsample=0.8,         # fraction of data per tree
    colsample_bytree=0.8,  # fraction of features per tree
    random_state=42,
    eval_metric="logloss"
)
model.fit(
    X_train, y_train,
    eval_set=[(X_test, y_test)],
    verbose=False
)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{K-Means Clustering (Unsupervised)}

\textbf{What it does:} Partitions data into $k$ clusters by iteratively assigning each point to the nearest cluster centre (centroid) and updating the centroids.

\textbf{How to choose $k$:} Use the Elbow Method---plot inertia vs.\,$k$ and look for the ``elbow'' where improvement slows.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={K-Means clustering with elbow method}]
from sklearn.cluster import KMeans
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Elbow method to find optimal k
inertias = []
for k in range(1, 11):
    km = KMeans(n_clusters=k, random_state=42, n_init=10)
    km.fit(X_scaled)
    inertias.append(km.inertia_)

plt.plot(range(1, 11), inertias, marker="o")
plt.xlabel("Number of clusters (k)")
plt.ylabel("Inertia")
plt.title("Elbow Method")
plt.show()

# Train with chosen k
kmeans = KMeans(n_clusters=3, random_state=42, n_init=10)
labels = kmeans.fit_predict(X_scaled)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Feature Engineering}

\textbf{Feature engineering} is the process of creating, transforming, or selecting features to improve model performance. Often more important than the choice of algorithm.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Feature engineering techniques}]
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from sklearn.preprocessing import (
    StandardScaler, MinMaxScaler, LabelEncoder,
    OneHotEncoder, PolynomialFeatures
)

# 1. Handle missing values
df["age"].fillna(df["age"].median(), inplace=True)
df["category"].fillna("unknown", inplace=True)
df.dropna(subset=["critical_column"], inplace=True)

# 2. Encode categorical variables
# Label encoding (ordinal: low < medium < high)
le = LabelEncoder()
df["size_encoded"] = le.fit_transform(df["size"])

# One-hot encoding (nominal: red, blue, green)
df = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=["colour"], drop_first=True)

# 3. Scale numerical features (CRITICAL for SVM, KNN, neural nets)
scaler = StandardScaler()  # mean=0, std=1
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

# 4. Create new features (domain knowledge!)
df["price_per_sqft"] = df["price"] / df["sqft"]
df["age_group"] = pd.cut(df["age"], bins=[0,18,35,65,100],
                         labels=["child","young","adult","senior"])

# 5. Log transform skewed distributions
df["log_price"] = np.log1p(df["price"])  # log(1+x) handles zeros
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
The golden rule of preprocessing: \textbf{fit on training data, transform on both.} Never fit your scaler/encoder on the test set---this causes data leakage.
\end{tipbox}

\section{Cross-Validation}

\textbf{Problem:} A single train/test split can give misleading results.

\textbf{Solution:} \textbf{K-Fold Cross-Validation} splits data into $k$ folds. For each fold, train on $k-1$ folds and test on the remaining one. Repeat $k$ times and average.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Cross-validation}]
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score, StratifiedKFold

# Basic cross-validation
scores = cross_val_score(
    model, X, y,
    cv=5,              # 5-fold CV
    scoring="accuracy"  # or "f1", "roc_auc"
)
print(f"CV Accuracy: {scores.mean():.4f} (+/- {scores.std():.4f})")

# Stratified K-Fold (preserves class distribution)
skf = StratifiedKFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42)
scores = cross_val_score(model, X, y, cv=skf, scoring="f1")
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Hyperparameter Tuning}

\textbf{Hyperparameters} are settings you choose \emph{before} training. The model does not learn these---you must tune them.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Hyperparameter tuning}]
from sklearn.model_selection import GridSearchCV

# Grid search: try every combination
param_grid = {
    "n_estimators": [50, 100, 200],
    "max_depth": [5, 10, 15, None],
    "min_samples_leaf": [1, 5, 10],
}

grid_search = GridSearchCV(
    RandomForestClassifier(random_state=42),
    param_grid,
    cv=5,
    scoring="f1",
    n_jobs=-1,
    verbose=1
)
grid_search.fit(X_train, y_train)

print(f"Best params: {grid_search.best_params_}")
print(f"Best F1: {grid_search.best_score_:.4f}")
best_model = grid_search.best_estimator_
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Complete ML Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Production ML pipeline}]
from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
from sklearn.compose import ColumnTransformer

numeric_features = ["age", "income", "score"]
categorical_features = ["category", "region"]

preprocessor = ColumnTransformer(
    transformers=[
        ("num", StandardScaler(), numeric_features),
        ("cat", OneHotEncoder(drop="first",
                              handle_unknown="ignore"),
         categorical_features),
    ]
)

# Create pipeline: preprocessing + model
pipeline = Pipeline([
    ("preprocessor", preprocessor),
    ("classifier", RandomForestClassifier(
        n_estimators=100, random_state=42
    )),
])

# Fit and predict (preprocessing happens automatically)
pipeline.fit(X_train, y_train)
y_pred = pipeline.predict(X_test)

# Cross-validate the entire pipeline (no data leakage!)
scores = cross_val_score(pipeline, X, y, cv=5, scoring="f1")

# Save pipeline for deployment
import joblib
joblib.dump(pipeline, "model_pipeline.joblib")
loaded = joblib.load("model_pipeline.joblib")
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Key Metrics (Detailed)}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Accuracy:} $\frac{TP + TN}{TP + TN + FP + FN}$. Misleading with imbalanced data.
    \item \textbf{Precision:} $\frac{TP}{TP + FP}$. ``Of all predicted positive, how many actually were?''
    \item \textbf{Recall (Sensitivity):} $\frac{TP}{TP + FN}$. ``Of all actual positives, how many did I catch?''
    \item \textbf{F1 Score:} $\frac{2 \times \text{Precision} \times \text{Recall}}{\text{Precision} + \text{Recall}}$. Best single metric for imbalanced data.
    \item \textbf{AUC-ROC:} Area under ROC curve. 1.0 = perfect, 0.5 = random.
    \item \textbf{Confusion Matrix:} 2x2 table showing TP, TN, FP, FN.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
In medical contexts (Breast Cancer Detection), \textbf{recall} is more important than precision. You would rather flag a healthy person for testing (false positive) than miss a cancer patient (false negative).
\end{warningbox}

\section{Bias, Variance, and Overfitting}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Overfitting:} Model too complex---memorises training data including noise. High train accuracy, low test accuracy.
    \item \textbf{Underfitting:} Model too simple---cannot capture patterns. Both train and test accuracy low.
    \item \textbf{Bias:} Error from incorrect assumptions. High bias = underfitting.
    \item \textbf{Variance:} Error from sensitivity to training data. High variance = overfitting.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Solutions to overfitting:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item More training data.
    \item Regularisation (L1/L2 penalties, dropout).
    \item Simpler model (fewer features, shallower tree).
    \item Cross-validation.
    \item Early stopping.
    \item Feature selection.
    \item Data augmentation.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{Breast Cancer Detection} project applies this entire workflow: load medical features, train a classifier, evaluate with precision/recall. Your \textbf{AgriXnest} project uses ML for plant identification.
\end{projectbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand ML deeply: supervised vs.\,unsupervised learning, 8 core algorithms (linear regression, logistic regression, decision trees, random forests, SVM, KNN, XGBoost, K-Means), feature engineering, cross-validation, hyperparameter tuning, evaluation metrics, bias-variance trade-off, and complete ML pipelines.}


\section{Chapter Review: Machine Learning}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item ML = teaching computers to learn patterns from data instead of explicit rules.
    \item \textbf{Supervised learning}: labelled data $\to$ classification or regression.
    \item \textbf{Random Forest} and \textbf{XGBoost} are the best default choices for tabular data.
    \item Always \textbf{scale features} for distance-based algorithms (SVM, KNN).
    \item Use \textbf{cross-validation} instead of a single train/test split.
    \item \textbf{F1 score} is better than accuracy for imbalanced datasets.
    \item Build \textbf{pipelines} to prevent data leakage and ensure reproducibility.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Quick Recall Questions}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between classification and regression?
    \item Explain the bias-variance trade-off in your own words.
    \item When would you use KNN vs.\,Random Forest vs.\,XGBoost?
    \item What is data leakage and how do pipelines prevent it?
    \item What is the difference between precision and recall? When is each more important?
    \item What is cross-validation and why is it better than a single split?
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Practical Exercises}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Load the Iris dataset and build a classifier with 3 different algorithms. Compare their accuracy.
    \item Build a complete ML pipeline with preprocessing, training, and evaluation.
    \item Use GridSearchCV to tune hyperparameters for a Random Forest model.
    \item Create a confusion matrix and classification report. Interpret the results.
\end{enumerate}



% CHAPTER 25 — DEEP LEARNING WITH TENSORFLOW
% ============================================================
\chapter{Deep Learning with TensorFlow}

\section{What Is Deep Learning?}

\textbf{Deep learning} is a subset of machine learning that uses \textbf{neural networks with many layers} (hence ``deep'') to learn complex patterns from data. While traditional ML requires you to manually engineer features, deep learning can learn features automatically from raw data (images, text, audio).

\textbf{Key differences from traditional ML:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Feature learning:} Deep networks learn features automatically. No manual feature engineering needed for images/text.
    \item \textbf{Scale:} Deep learning performance improves with more data; traditional ML plateaus.
    \item \textbf{Compute:} Requires GPUs for training. CPUs are too slow for large models.
    \item \textbf{Data hungry:} Needs large datasets. With small data, traditional ML often wins.
\end{itemize}

\section{Neural Network Fundamentals}

A \textbf{neural network} is a function composed of layers of interconnected nodes (neurons). Each neuron computes:

$$\text{output} = \text{activation}(\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{x} + b)$$

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Weights ($\mathbf{w}$):} Learnable parameters that determine input importance. Initialised randomly, updated during training.
    \item \textbf{Bias ($b$):} A learnable offset that shifts the activation function.
    \item \textbf{Activation function:} Introduces non-linearity. Without it, stacking layers would be equivalent to a single linear transformation.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{ReLU} (Rectified Linear Unit): $f(x) = \max(0, x)$. Most common. Fast, avoids vanishing gradient.
        \item \textbf{Sigmoid:} $f(x) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-x}}$. Output 0--1. Used in binary classification output layer.
        \item \textbf{Softmax:} Generalises sigmoid to multiple classes. Outputs a probability distribution summing to 1.
        \item \textbf{Tanh:} $f(x) = \frac{e^x - e^{-x}}{e^x + e^{-x}}$. Output $-1$ to $1$. Used in RNNs.
        \item \textbf{Leaky ReLU:} $f(x) = x$ if $x > 0$, else $0.01x$. Fixes ``dying ReLU'' problem where neurons output 0 forever.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Loss function:} Measures how wrong predictions are. The goal of training is to minimise this.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Binary cross-entropy:} For binary classification. $L = -[y \log(\hat{y}) + (1-y)\log(1-\hat{y})]$.
        \item \textbf{Categorical cross-entropy:} For multi-class classification.
        \item \textbf{Mean Squared Error (MSE):} For regression. $L = \frac{1}{n}\sum(y - \hat{y})^2$.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Optimiser:} Algorithm that updates weights to minimise loss.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{SGD (Stochastic Gradient Descent):} Simple but needs careful learning rate tuning.
        \item \textbf{Adam:} Adaptive Moment Estimation. \textbf{The default choice}---combines momentum and adaptive learning rates. Use this unless you have a reason not to.
        \item \textbf{Learning rate:} How big each weight update step is. Too high = oscillate and never converge. Too low = train forever. Typical starting value: $10^{-3}$ for Adam.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Backpropagation:} Computes how much each weight contributed to the error (gradients), so the optimiser knows how to adjust them. Uses the chain rule from calculus.
    \item \textbf{Epoch:} One complete pass through the entire training dataset.
    \item \textbf{Batch size:} Number of samples processed before weights are updated. Common: 32, 64, 128. Larger batches = faster but may generalise worse.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Network Architecture Types}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Feedforward / Dense / MLP (Multi-Layer Perceptron):} Layers connected sequentially. Each neuron connects to every neuron in the next layer. Good for tabular data.
    \item \textbf{Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs):} Designed for spatial data (images). Use learnable filters that slide over the image detecting patterns (edges, textures, shapes). This chapter focuses on CNNs.
    \item \textbf{Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs):} Designed for sequential data (text, time series). Have memory of previous inputs through recurrent connections. Problem: suffer from vanishing gradients on long sequences.
    \item \textbf{Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM):} Improved RNN with ``gates'' that control what information to remember, forget, and output. Solves the vanishing gradient problem.
    \item \textbf{Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU):} Simplified LSTM with fewer parameters. Often performs comparably.
    \item \textbf{Transformers:} Modern architecture using \textbf{attention mechanisms} instead of recurrence. Processes entire sequences in parallel. Powers GPT, BERT, and all modern LLMs. Covered in the next chapter.
    \item \textbf{Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs):} Two networks compete: a \emph{generator} creates fake data while a \emph{discriminator} tries to distinguish fake from real. Used for image generation, style transfer, data augmentation.
    \item \textbf{Autoencoders:} Networks that learn compressed representations by encoding input to a bottleneck layer then decoding back. Used for dimensionality reduction, anomaly detection, denoising.
\end{itemize}

\section{Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs) In Depth}

CNNs are the backbone of computer vision. They learn hierarchical features:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Early layers:} Detect edges, corners, simple colour gradients.
    \item \textbf{Middle layers:} Detect shapes, parts of objects (eyes, wheels, leaves).
    \item \textbf{Deep layers:} Detect entire objects or complex patterns.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Key CNN operations:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Convolution (Conv2D):} A small learnable filter (e.g., $3 \times 3$) slides across the image, computing dot products to produce a \emph{feature map}. Each filter detects different patterns. Parameters: number of filters, kernel size, stride, padding.
    \item \textbf{Padding:} Adding zeros around the image border.\ \texttt{padding="same"} keeps spatial dimensions; \texttt{padding="valid"} (default) shrinks them.
    \item \textbf{Stride:} How many pixels the filter moves per step. Stride 2 halves the spatial dimensions.
    \item \textbf{MaxPooling2D:} Downsamples by taking the maximum value in each $2\times2$ region. Reduces dimensions, adds translation invariance.
    \item \textbf{GlobalAveragePooling2D:} Averages each entire feature map to a single number. Modern alternative to Flatten + Dense.
    \item \textbf{Flatten:} Converts 2D feature maps to 1D vector for the dense classification layers.
    \item \textbf{Dropout:} Randomly sets a fraction (e.g., 50\%) of neurons to zero during training. Prevents overfitting by forcing redundant learning.
    \item \textbf{BatchNormalization:} Normalises layer inputs to have zero mean and unit variance. Stabilises training, allows higher learning rates, acts as mild regularisation.
\end{itemize}

\section{TensorFlow / Keras Workflow}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Complete CNN with data augmentation and callbacks}]
import tensorflow as tf
from tensorflow import keras
from tensorflow.keras import layers

# 1. Load and preprocess data
(x_train, y_train), (x_test, y_test) = (
    keras.datasets.cifar10.load_data()
)
x_train = x_train.astype("float32") / 255.0  # normalise to [0,1]
x_test = x_test.astype("float32") / 255.0

# 2. Data augmentation (create variations of training images)
# This artificially increases dataset size and reduces overfitting
data_augmentation = keras.Sequential([
    layers.RandomFlip("horizontal"),    # mirror images
    layers.RandomRotation(0.1),         # rotate up to 10%
    layers.RandomZoom(0.1),             # zoom up to 10%
    layers.RandomContrast(0.1),         # vary contrast
])

# 3. Build model (CNN with batch normalisation)
model = keras.Sequential([
    # Augmentation (only active during training)
    data_augmentation,

    # Block 1: learn low-level features (edges, textures)
    layers.Conv2D(32, (3,3), padding="same",
                  input_shape=(32, 32, 3)),
    layers.BatchNormalization(),
    layers.Activation("relu"),
    layers.MaxPooling2D((2, 2)),

    # Block 2: learn mid-level features (shapes, parts)
    layers.Conv2D(64, (3,3), padding="same"),
    layers.BatchNormalization(),
    layers.Activation("relu"),
    layers.MaxPooling2D((2, 2)),

    # Block 3: learn high-level features (objects)
    layers.Conv2D(128, (3,3), padding="same"),
    layers.BatchNormalization(),
    layers.Activation("relu"),
    layers.GlobalAveragePooling2D(),

    # Classification head
    layers.Dense(256, activation="relu"),
    layers.Dropout(0.5),  # 50% dropout to prevent overfitting
    layers.Dense(10, activation="softmax"),  # 10 classes
])

# 4. Compile
model.compile(
    optimizer=keras.optimizers.Adam(learning_rate=1e-3),
    loss="sparse_categorical_crossentropy",
    metrics=["accuracy"],
)

# 5. Print model architecture summary
model.summary()
# Shows layer names, output shapes, parameter counts
# Total params tells you how big the model is

# 6. Train with callbacks
callbacks = [
    # Stop training if validation loss doesn't improve for 5 epochs
    keras.callbacks.EarlyStopping(
        patience=5, restore_best_weights=True
    ),
    # Reduce learning rate when validation loss plateaus
    keras.callbacks.ReduceLROnPlateau(
        factor=0.5, patience=3, min_lr=1e-6
    ),
    # Save the best model during training
    keras.callbacks.ModelCheckpoint(
        "best_model.keras", save_best_only=True
    ),
]

history = model.fit(
    x_train, y_train,
    epochs=50,            # max epochs (early stopping may cut short)
    batch_size=64,
    validation_split=0.2, # use 20% of training data for validation
    callbacks=callbacks,
)

# 7. Evaluate on test set
test_loss, test_acc = model.evaluate(x_test, y_test)
print(f"Test accuracy: {test_acc:.4f}")

# 8. Plot training history to diagnose over/underfitting
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

fig, (ax1, ax2) = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))
ax1.plot(history.history["loss"], label="Train Loss")
ax1.plot(history.history["val_loss"], label="Validation Loss")
ax1.set_title("Loss Curves")
ax1.legend()
# If train loss << val loss  ->  OVERFITTING
# If both losses are high    ->  UNDERFITTING

ax2.plot(history.history["accuracy"], label="Train Accuracy")
ax2.plot(history.history["val_accuracy"], label="Val Accuracy")
ax2.set_title("Accuracy Curves")
ax2.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("training_history.png")
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{How to read training curves:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Train and val curves close together and both improving = good.
    \item Train improving but val not = overfitting. Add dropout, data augmentation, or more data.
    \item Both curves plateau at poor performance = underfitting. Use a bigger model or train longer.
\end{itemize}
\end{notebox}

\section{Transfer Learning}

\textbf{Transfer learning} uses a model pre-trained on a large dataset (like ImageNet with 14 million images) and adapts it to your specific task. This is the \textbf{standard approach} when you have limited data.

\textbf{Why it works:} Early CNN layers learn universal features (edges, textures, colours) that transfer across tasks. You only need to retrain the final classification layers for your specific problem.

\textbf{Pre-trained models available in Keras:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{ResNet50/101/152:} Residual networks with skip connections. Your Breast Cancer project uses ResNet-18.
    \item \textbf{VGG16/19:} Simple, deep architecture. Good for learning but slower.
    \item \textbf{EfficientNet:} Best accuracy-to-compute ratio. Modern choice.
    \item \textbf{MobileNet:} Lightweight, designed for mobile/edge devices.
    \item \textbf{InceptionV3:} Multiple filter sizes per layer. Captures features at different scales.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Transfer learning with ResNet50}]
# 1. Load pre-trained ResNet50 (without top classification layer)
base_model = keras.applications.ResNet50(
    weights="imagenet",      # pre-trained on ImageNet
    include_top=False,       # remove original classification head
    input_shape=(224, 224, 3),
)

# 2. Freeze pre-trained layers (don't update their weights)
base_model.trainable = False

# 3. Add your own classification head
model = keras.Sequential([
    base_model,
    layers.GlobalAveragePooling2D(),
    layers.Dense(256, activation="relu"),
    layers.Dropout(0.5),
    layers.Dense(2, activation="softmax"),  # benign/malignant
])

# 4. Phase 1: Train only the new layers (fast, ~5 minutes)
model.compile(optimizer="adam",
              loss="sparse_categorical_crossentropy",
              metrics=["accuracy"])
model.fit(X_train, y_train, epochs=10, validation_split=0.2)

# 5. Phase 2: Fine-tune -- unfreeze top layers and train slowly
base_model.trainable = True
for layer in base_model.layers[:-20]:
    layer.trainable = False  # keep early layers frozen

model.compile(
    optimizer=keras.optimizers.Adam(1e-5),  # very low LR!
    loss="sparse_categorical_crossentropy",
    metrics=["accuracy"]
)
model.fit(X_train, y_train, epochs=10, validation_split=0.2)
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{warningbox}
When fine-tuning, always use a \textbf{very low learning rate} ($10^{-5}$). High learning rates will destroy the pre-trained weights. Also, always fine-tune \emph{after} training the new head---never unfreeze and train from the start.
\end{warningbox}

\section{ResNet: Residual Networks}

\textbf{ResNet} introduced \textbf{skip connections} (residual connections) that allow gradients to flow directly through the network, solving the vanishing gradient problem in very deep networks.

\textbf{Key innovation:} Instead of learning the output $H(x)$ directly, ResNet layers learn the \emph{residual} $F(x) = H(x) - x$, and the output is $H(x) = F(x) + x$. The ``$+ x$'' is the skip connection.

\textbf{Why it matters:} Before ResNet, networks deeper than $\sim$20 layers performed \emph{worse} than shallower ones due to vanishing gradients. ResNet enabled training networks 100+ layers deep. Your Breast Cancer Detection project uses ResNet-18.

\section{Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) and LSTMs}

\textbf{RNNs} process sequences one element at a time, maintaining a hidden state that captures information about previous elements.

\textbf{Problem:} Standard RNNs cannot remember long-range dependencies due to vanishing gradients. By the time you process the 100th word, information about the 1st word has been ``washed out''.

\textbf{LSTM (Long Short-Term Memory)} solves this with three ``gates'':
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Forget gate:} Decides what information to throw away from the cell state.
    \item \textbf{Input gate:} Decides what new information to store in the cell state.
    \item \textbf{Output gate:} Decides what to output based on the cell state.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={LSTM for text classification}]
# LSTM for sentiment analysis
model = keras.Sequential([
    layers.Embedding(vocab_size, 128, input_length=max_length),
    layers.Bidirectional(layers.LSTM(64, return_sequences=True)),
    layers.Bidirectional(layers.LSTM(32)),
    layers.Dense(64, activation="relu"),
    layers.Dropout(0.5),
    layers.Dense(1, activation="sigmoid"),  # binary sentiment
])

model.compile(
    optimizer="adam",
    loss="binary_crossentropy",
    metrics=["accuracy"]
)
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{Bidirectional} LSTMs process sequences in both directions (left-to-right and right-to-left), capturing context from both sides. This is almost always better than unidirectional. However, Transformers (next chapter) have largely replaced LSTMs for most NLP tasks due to their ability to process sequences in parallel.
\end{notebox}

\section{Model Saving and Deployment}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Saving and loading models}]
# Save entire model (architecture + weights + optimizer)
model.save("my_model.keras")  # Keras 3 format

# Load model
loaded_model = keras.models.load_model("my_model.keras")

# Save only weights (for sharing or fine-tuning)
model.save_weights("model_weights.weights.h5")
model.load_weights("model_weights.weights.h5")

# Export for production serving
model.export("exported_model")  # TensorFlow SavedModel format
# Serve with TensorFlow Serving, or convert to TFLite for mobile

# Convert to TFLite for mobile deployment
converter = tf.lite.TFLiteConverter.from_saved_model("exported_model")
tflite_model = converter.convert()
with open("model.tflite", "wb") as f:
    f.write(tflite_model)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Practical Tips for Deep Learning}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Start with transfer learning.} Do not train from scratch unless you have millions of data points.
    \item \textbf{Use data augmentation.} It is free extra data.
    \item \textbf{Use batch normalisation.} It stabilises and accelerates training.
    \item \textbf{Use dropout} (0.2--0.5) in dense layers to prevent overfitting.
    \item \textbf{Monitor validation loss}, not training loss. Training loss will always decrease.
    \item \textbf{Use early stopping.} Stop training when validation loss stops improving.
    \item \textbf{Start with Adam optimiser} and learning rate $10^{-3}$.
    \item \textbf{Use GPU.} Training on CPU is 10--50x slower. Google Colab offers free GPUs.
    \item \textbf{Normalise inputs} to [0, 1] or zero mean/unit variance.
    \item \textbf{Class imbalance:} Use class weights, oversampling (SMOTE), or focal loss.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{Breast Cancer Detection} project uses ResNet-18 with transfer learning to classify medical images as benign/malignant. Your \textbf{AgriXnest} project uses CNNs for plant identification. Both follow this workflow: load pre-trained model, add classification head, fine-tune on domain data, evaluate with recall-focused metrics.
\end{projectbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand deep learning comprehensively: neural network internals (weights, biases, activations, loss, optimisers, backpropagation), CNN architecture and operations (convolution, pooling, batch norm, dropout), the full TensorFlow/Keras workflow with data augmentation and callbacks, transfer learning with ResNet, RNNs and LSTMs for sequences, model saving and deployment, and practical tips. This connects directly to your Breast Cancer Detection and AgriXnest projects.}


\section{Chapter Review: Deep Learning}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A \textbf{neural network} is a function of layered matrix multiplications with non-linear activations.
    \item \textbf{CNNs} are the standard for image tasks; they learn hierarchical features (edges $\to$ shapes $\to$ objects).
    \item \textbf{Transfer learning} is the default approach: use a pre-trained model and fine-tune.
    \item \textbf{Data augmentation}, \textbf{dropout}, and \textbf{early stopping} are your main tools against overfitting.
    \item \textbf{RNNs/LSTMs} handle sequences but are being replaced by Transformers for most tasks.
    \item Always monitor \textbf{validation loss}, not training loss.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Quick Recall Questions}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the purpose of an activation function? Why can't we stack linear layers without one?
    \item Name three types of neural network architectures and their best use cases.
    \item What is the difference between MaxPooling and GlobalAveragePooling?
    \item Explain transfer learning in two sentences.
    \item What is the vanishing gradient problem and how does ResNet solve it?
    \item Why do we use a very low learning rate when fine-tuning?
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Practical Exercises}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Build a CNN to classify handwritten digits (MNIST dataset) from scratch.
    \item Use transfer learning with MobileNet to classify 5 types of flowers.
    \item Train the same model with and without data augmentation. Compare the results.
    \item Plot training curves and identify overfitting vs.\,underfitting.
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 26 — NLP + SPACY
% ============================================================
\chapter{NLP + spaCy}

\section{What Is NLP?}

\textbf{Natural Language Processing (NLP)} is the branch of AI that teaches computers to understand, interpret, and generate human language. It bridges the gap between human communication and computer understanding.

\textbf{Why is NLP hard?} Language is inherently ambiguous. ``I saw her duck'' could mean you saw her pet duck, or you saw her crouch down. Sarcasm, slang, context, and cultural references make NLP one of the hardest AI problems.

\subsection{NLP Tasks}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Tokenization:} Splitting text into meaningful units (words, subwords, characters). ``Hello world'' $\to$ [``Hello'', ``world''].
    \item \textbf{Lemmatization:} Reducing words to their base/dictionary form. ``running'', ``ran'', ``runs'' $\to$ ``run''. Preserves meaning.
    \item \textbf{Stemming:} Cruder version---chops word endings. ``running'' $\to$ ``runn''. Less accurate but faster.
    \item \textbf{Stop word removal:} Removing common words that carry little meaning (``the'', ``is'', ``at'').
    \item \textbf{Named Entity Recognition (NER):} Identifying entities in text: people, places, organisations, dates, monetary values.
    \item \textbf{Part-of-Speech (POS) tagging:} Labelling each word with its grammatical role (noun, verb, adjective).
    \item \textbf{Sentiment analysis:} Determining emotional tone (positive, negative, neutral).
    \item \textbf{Text classification:} Assigning categories to text (spam detection, topic classification).
    \item \textbf{Text summarization:} Condensing long text while preserving key information.
    \item \textbf{Machine translation:} Translating text between languages.
    \item \textbf{Question answering:} Answering questions based on a given text passage.
    \item \textbf{Text generation:} Producing new text (what LLMs like GPT do).
\end{itemize}

\section{Text Preprocessing Pipeline}

Before feeding text to any ML model, you must preprocess it:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Complete NLP preprocessing pipeline}]
import re
import spacy

nlp = spacy.load("en_core_web_sm")

def preprocess_text(text):
    # 1. Lowercase
    text = text.lower()

    # 2. Remove URLs
    text = re.sub(r"https?://\S+|www\.\S+", "", text)

    # 3. Remove HTML tags
    text = re.sub(r"<.*?>", "", text)

    # 4. Remove special characters (keep letters/numbers/spaces)
    text = re.sub(r"[^a-zA-Z0-9\s]", "", text)

    # 5. Process with spaCy (tokenize, lemmatize, remove stops)
    doc = nlp(text)
    tokens = [
        token.lemma_           # lemmatized form
        for token in doc
        if not token.is_stop   # remove stop words
        and not token.is_punct # remove punctuation
        and len(token.text) > 1  # remove single characters
    ]

    return " ".join(tokens)

# Example
raw = "The engineers were running multiple tests at Google's HQ!"
clean = preprocess_text(raw)
print(clean)  # "engineer run multiple test google hq"
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Text Representation: From Words to Numbers}

ML models need numbers, not words. Here are the approaches to converting text into numerical vectors:

\subsection{Bag of Words (BoW)}

\textbf{Idea:} Represent each document as a vector of word counts. Ignores word order completely.

\textbf{Limitation:} Common words dominate. ``the'' appears 100 times but tells you nothing.

\subsection{TF-IDF (Term Frequency--Inverse Document Frequency)}

\textbf{TF-IDF} improves on BoW by weighing each word by its importance:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{TF (Term Frequency):} How often the word appears in this document.
    \item \textbf{IDF (Inverse Document Frequency):} $\log\frac{\text{total documents}}{\text{documents containing this word}}$. Rare words get higher weight.
    \item \textbf{TF-IDF = TF $\times$ IDF.} Words frequent in one document but rare overall get the highest scores.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Bag of Words and TF-IDF}]
from sklearn.feature_extraction.text import (
    CountVectorizer, TfidfVectorizer
)

corpus = [
    "I love machine learning",
    "Machine learning is amazing",
    "Deep learning is a subset of machine learning"
]

# Bag of Words (raw word counts)
bow = CountVectorizer()
X_bow = bow.fit_transform(corpus)
print(bow.get_feature_names_out())
# ['amazing', 'deep', 'is', 'learning', 'love', 'machine', ...]

# TF-IDF (weighted word importance -- preferred)
tfidf = TfidfVectorizer(
    max_features=5000,     # limit vocabulary size
    ngram_range=(1, 2),    # unigrams AND bigrams
    min_df=2,              # ignore very rare words
    max_df=0.95            # ignore very common words
)
X_tfidf = tfidf.fit_transform(corpus)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Word Embeddings}

\textbf{Problem with BoW/TF-IDF:} They treat words as independent. ``king'' and ``queen'' are as different as ``king'' and ``banana''. No semantic understanding.

\textbf{Word embeddings} represent words as dense vectors in a high-dimensional space where \emph{similar words have similar vectors}.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Word2Vec:} Trained by predicting context words. Captures semantic relationships: $\text{king} - \text{man} + \text{woman} \approx \text{queen}$.
    \item \textbf{GloVe:} Global Vectors. Trained on word co-occurrence statistics.
    \item \textbf{FastText:} Like Word2Vec but works on subword units. Can handle out-of-vocabulary words.
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
Modern NLP has largely moved from static word embeddings (Word2Vec, GloVe) to \textbf{contextual embeddings} (BERT, GPT) where the same word gets different vectors depending on context. ``bank'' in ``river bank'' gets a different vector than ``bank'' in ``bank account''. This is covered in the Transformers chapter.
\end{notebox}

\section{spaCy: Industrial-Strength NLP}

\textbf{spaCy} is a production-ready NLP library. It is fast, memory-efficient, and designed for real-world use (not research).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Comprehensive spaCy example}]
import spacy

# Load model (sm=small, md=medium with word vectors, lg=large)
# Install: python -m spacy download en_core_web_sm
nlp = spacy.load("en_core_web_sm")

# Process text -- this runs the entire NLP pipeline
text = ("Amzat Karim is a software engineer based in London. "
        "He works on RevSync, a motorcycle ECU tuning platform "
        "built with Django and React Native.")
doc = nlp(text)

# ---------- TOKENIZATION + POS + DEPENDENCY ----------
print("=== Tokens ===")
for token in doc[:10]:  # first 10 tokens
    print(f"  {token.text:15} POS={token.pos_:8} "
          f"DEP={token.dep_:12} LEMMA={token.lemma_}")

# ---------- NAMED ENTITY RECOGNITION ----------
print("\n=== Named Entities ===")
for ent in doc.ents:
    print(f"  {ent.text:20} LABEL={ent.label_:10} "
          f"({spacy.explain(ent.label_)})")
# Amzat Karim         PERSON     (People, incl. fictional)
# London              GPE        (Countries, cities, states)
# RevSync             ORG        (Companies, agencies)

# ---------- NOUN CHUNKS (noun phrases) ----------
print("\n=== Noun Chunks ===")
for chunk in doc.noun_chunks:
    print(f"  {chunk.text:30} ROOT={chunk.root.text}")

# ---------- SENTENCE DETECTION ----------
print("\n=== Sentences ===")
for sent in doc.sents:
    print(f"  - {sent.text}")

# ---------- SIMILARITY (needs md or lg model) ----------
doc1 = nlp("motorcycle tuning")
doc2 = nlp("ECU modification")
doc3 = nlp("cooking recipes")
print(f"\n'motorcycle tuning' vs 'ECU modification': "
      f"{doc1.similarity(doc2):.4f}")
print(f"'motorcycle tuning' vs 'cooking recipes': "
      f"{doc1.similarity(doc3):.4f}")
# First pair should have HIGHER similarity
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Text Classification Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Sentiment analysis with TF-IDF + classifier}]
from sklearn.feature_extraction.text import TfidfVectorizer
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score

# Sample labelled data
texts = [
    "This product is amazing, I love it!",
    "Terrible quality, complete waste of money",
    "Works great, highly recommend",
    "Broke after one day, very disappointed",
    "Best purchase I have ever made",
    "Do not buy this, horrible experience",
]
labels = [1, 0, 1, 0, 1, 0]  # 1=positive, 0=negative

# Build pipeline: TF-IDF vectorizer + classifier
text_clf = Pipeline([
    ("tfidf", TfidfVectorizer(
        max_features=10000,
        ngram_range=(1, 2),    # unigrams and bigrams
        min_df=2,
        max_df=0.95,
    )),
    ("clf", LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)),
])

# Cross-validate
scores = cross_val_score(text_clf, texts, labels,
                         cv=3, scoring="f1")
print(f"F1 Score: {scores.mean():.4f}")

# Train and predict
text_clf.fit(texts, labels)
new_text = ["This is the best thing I have ever bought!"]
prediction = text_clf.predict(new_text)
print(f"Sentiment: {'Positive' if prediction[0] == 1 else 'Negative'}")
\end{lstlisting}

\section{BERT for NLP (Overview)}

\textbf{BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers)} is a pre-trained language model from Google that revolutionised NLP in 2018. It reads text \emph{bidirectionally} (both left-to-right and right-to-left) to understand context.

\textbf{How to use BERT:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Start with pre-trained BERT (trained on massive text corpus).
    \item Add a task-specific classification head on top.
    \item Fine-tune on your labelled data.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={BERT for text classification with Hugging Face}]
# pip install transformers torch
from transformers import pipeline

# Zero-shot classification (no training needed!)
classifier = pipeline("zero-shot-classification",
                      model="facebook/bart-large-mnli")

text = "RevSync allows motorcycle riders to flash ECU tunes"
labels = ["technology", "sports", "cooking", "finance"]

result = classifier(text, labels)
print(result["labels"][0])   # "technology"
print(result["scores"][0])   # 0.94 (confidence)

# Sentiment analysis (pre-trained model)
sentiment = pipeline("sentiment-analysis")
result = sentiment("This motorcycle tune runs perfectly!")
print(result)  # [{'label': 'POSITIVE', 'score': 0.9998}]

# Named entity recognition
ner = pipeline("ner", grouped_entities=True)
result = ner("Amzat Karim works at RevSync in London")
for entity in result:
    print(f"{entity['word']:15} {entity['entity_group']:10} "
          f"score={entity['score']:.4f}")
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
The \textbf{Hugging Face Transformers} library provides thousands of pre-trained models for every NLP task. Before building your own model, check if a pre-trained one already exists. For most tasks, fine-tuning a pre-trained model beats training from scratch.
\end{tipbox}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{CareerForge AI} uses NLP for resume parsing and skill extraction---this involves NER (extracting names, companies, skills), text classification (categorising job roles), and structured information extraction. Your \textbf{VeriRAG} system uses NLP for claim decomposition and fact-checking analysis.
\end{projectbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand NLP comprehensively: the full preprocessing pipeline, text representation (BoW, TF-IDF, word embeddings), spaCy for industrial NLP (tokenization, NER, POS tagging, similarity), text classification pipelines, and BERT/Transformers for modern NLP. This connects to your CareerForge AI and VeriRAG projects.}


\section{Chapter Review: NLP}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item NLP = teaching computers to understand human language.
    \item Text must be \textbf{preprocessed} (lowercase, remove stops, lemmatize) before ML.
    \item \textbf{TF-IDF} is better than raw word counts for text representation.
    \item \textbf{Word embeddings} capture semantic meaning (similar words = similar vectors).
    \item \textbf{spaCy} is the go-to library for production NLP (tokenization, NER, POS).
    \item Modern NLP uses \textbf{contextual embeddings} (BERT) that understand word meaning in context.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Quick Recall Questions}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between stemming and lemmatization?
    \item Explain TF-IDF in plain English.
    \item Why are word embeddings better than bag-of-words?
    \item What is Named Entity Recognition? Give three entity types.
    \item What is the difference between static embeddings (Word2Vec) and contextual embeddings (BERT)?
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Practical Exercises}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Build a spam classifier using TF-IDF + Logistic Regression.
    \item Use spaCy to extract all named entities from a news article.
    \item Compare the performance of BoW vs.\,TF-IDF on a text classification task.
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 27 — LLM INTEGRATION + RAG + VERIFICATION


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER: TRANSFORMERS AND MODERN AI
% ============================================================
\chapter{Transformers and Modern AI}

This chapter covers the most important architecture in modern AI: the \textbf{Transformer}. Every large language model (GPT, Claude, Gemini, Llama, BERT) is built on Transformers. Understanding this architecture is essential for any AI engineer.

\section{The Problem Transformers Solve}

Before Transformers (introduced in the 2017 paper ``Attention Is All You Need''), sequential models like RNNs and LSTMs processed text one word at a time. This had two major problems:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Slow training:} Words must be processed sequentially---you cannot parallelise.
    \item \textbf{Long-range dependencies:} By the time you reach word 500, information about word 1 has been ``washed out'' through hundreds of sequential steps.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Transformers solve both problems} by processing all words simultaneously using \textbf{attention mechanisms} instead of recurrence. This enables massive parallelisation on GPUs and direct connections between any two words regardless of distance.

\section{Self-Attention: The Core Innovation}

\textbf{Self-attention} allows each word to ``look at'' every other word in the sentence and decide how much to pay attention to each one.

\textbf{Intuition:} Consider the sentence ``The cat sat on the mat because \emph{it} was comfortable.'' What does ``it'' refer to---the cat or the mat? A human reads the full context to decide. Self-attention does the same: the word ``it'' attends to all other words and learns that ``mat'' (or ``cat'') is the most relevant.

\subsection{Queries, Keys, and Values}

Self-attention works by analogy to a \textbf{search engine}:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Query (Q):} What am I looking for? (Like a search query.)
    \item \textbf{Key (K):} What information do I contain? (Like a page title.)
    \item \textbf{Value (V):} What is my actual content? (Like the page content.)
\end{itemize}

For each word:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Create a Query, Key, and Value vector by multiplying the word embedding by learned weight matrices: $Q = X W_Q$, $K = X W_K$, $V = X W_V$.
    \item Compute attention scores: how relevant is each other word? $\text{score} = Q \cdot K^T$.
    \item Scale: divide by $\sqrt{d_k}$ (dimension of keys) to prevent very large values.
    \item Apply softmax: convert scores to probabilities that sum to 1.
    \item Multiply by Values: weighted sum of content based on attention scores.
\end{enumerate}

$$\text{Attention}(Q, K, V) = \text{softmax}\left(\frac{QK^T}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) V$$

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Simplified self-attention in NumPy}]
import numpy as np

def softmax(x):
    e_x = np.exp(x - np.max(x, axis=-1, keepdims=True))
    return e_x / e_x.sum(axis=-1, keepdims=True)

# Simplified self-attention
# 4 words, each represented as a 3-dimensional embedding
X = np.array([
    [1.0, 0.0, 1.0],   # "The"
    [0.0, 1.0, 0.0],   # "cat"
    [1.0, 1.0, 0.0],   # "sat"
    [0.0, 0.0, 1.0],   # "down"
])

d_k = 3  # dimension

# In real Transformers, Q/K/V are computed via learned weight matrices
# Here we use X directly for simplicity
Q = K = V = X

# Step 1: Compute attention scores
scores = Q @ K.T  # (4, 4) matrix: every word's similarity to every other

# Step 2: Scale
scores = scores / np.sqrt(d_k)

# Step 3: Softmax (convert to probabilities)
attention_weights = softmax(scores)
print("Attention weights (each row sums to 1):")
print(attention_weights.round(3))

# Step 4: Weighted sum of values
output = attention_weights @ V  # (4, 3): each word's new representation
print("\nOutput (context-aware embeddings):")
print(output.round(3))
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Multi-Head Attention}

Instead of computing attention once, Transformers compute it \textbf{multiple times in parallel} (multiple ``heads''), each learning to focus on different types of relationships:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Head 1 might learn syntactic relationships (subject-verb agreement).
    \item Head 2 might learn semantic relationships (pronouns and their referents).
    \item Head 3 might learn positional relationships (adjacent words).
\end{itemize}

The outputs of all heads are concatenated and linearly transformed.

\section{Transformer Architecture}

A full Transformer has two main parts:

\subsection{Encoder}

Processes the input sequence and creates rich, context-aware representations. Used in models like \textbf{BERT}. Each encoder layer has:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Multi-head self-attention (each word attends to all other words).
    \item Add \& normalise (residual connection + layer normalisation).
    \item Feed-forward network (two dense layers with ReLU).
    \item Add \& normalise.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Decoder}

Generates output one token at a time. Used in models like \textbf{GPT}. Each decoder layer has:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Masked} multi-head self-attention (can only attend to previous tokens---prevents ``seeing the future'').
    \item Cross-attention (attends to the encoder output---only in encoder-decoder models like the original Transformer for translation).
    \item Feed-forward network.
    \item Add \& normalise after each sub-layer.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Positional Encoding}

Since Transformers process all tokens simultaneously (no sequential order), they need a way to know word positions. \textbf{Positional encodings} are vectors added to word embeddings that encode position information using sine and cosine functions of different frequencies.

\section{BERT vs.\,GPT}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{BERT} & \textbf{GPT} \\
\midrule
Architecture & Encoder only & Decoder only \\
Direction & Bidirectional (sees all context) & Left-to-right (autoregressive) \\
Training task & Masked language modelling & Next token prediction \\
Best for & Understanding (classification, NER) & Generation (text, code, chat) \\
Attention & Full (all words see all words) & Causal/masked (can't see future) \\
Use pattern & Fine-tune for specific tasks & Prompt engineering / fine-tune \\
Examples & BERT, RoBERTa, DeBERTa & GPT-3, GPT-4, Llama, Mistral \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{Encoder-decoder models} (like the original Transformer, T5, BART) use both parts and are best for sequence-to-sequence tasks: translation, summarisation, question answering.
\end{notebox}

\section{Training vs.\,Inference}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Pre-training:} Train the model on a massive text corpus (billions of tokens) to learn language, facts, and reasoning. Extremely expensive (millions of dollars, weeks on thousands of GPUs).
    \item \textbf{Fine-tuning:} Take a pre-trained model and train it further on a smaller, task-specific dataset. Much cheaper. Example: fine-tune GPT on customer support conversations.
    \item \textbf{Instruction tuning:} Fine-tune with instruction-response pairs to make the model follow instructions and be helpful. This is what makes ChatGPT a ``chat'' model rather than a text completion model.
    \item \textbf{RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback):} Further refine the model using human preferences. Human raters rank model outputs, and the model learns to produce outputs humans prefer.
    \item \textbf{Inference:} Using a trained model to generate predictions. This is what happens when you send a prompt to ChatGPT.
\end{itemize}

\section{Fine-Tuning vs.\,Prompting}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
& \textbf{Prompting} & \textbf{Fine-tuning} \\
\midrule
What changes & Only the input text & Model weights \\
Cost & API cost per request & GPU compute for training \\
Data needed & 0--20 examples in prompt & 100--100,000+ labelled examples \\
Customisation & Limited by context window & Deep behavioural changes \\
Latency & May be slower (long prompts) & Faster (no extra context) \\
When to use & Quick experiments, general tasks & Domain-specific, production apps \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Start with prompting.} Only fine-tune if prompting does not meet your quality requirements and you have sufficient labelled data.

\section{Model Scale and Parameters}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Parameters:} The learnable weights in the model. GPT-3 has 175 billion. GPT-4 is estimated at $> 1$ trillion.
    \item \textbf{Tokens:} The units the model processes. Words are split into subword tokens (``understanding'' $\to$ [``under'', ``stand'', ``ing'']). Most models have a context window (4K, 8K, 32K, 128K, 1M tokens).
    \item \textbf{Scaling laws:} As models get bigger (more parameters + more data + more compute), performance improves predictably. This is why companies invest billions in training larger models.
\end{itemize}

\section{AI Ethics, Bias, and Safety}

As an AI engineer, you have a responsibility to build systems that are safe, fair, and transparent:

\subsection{Bias}

LLMs can reflect and amplify biases from their training data:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Gender bias:} ``The nurse said she...'' vs.\,``The engineer said he...''
    \item \textbf{Racial bias:} Different treatment of names or dialects.
    \item \textbf{Cultural bias:} Overrepresenting Western perspectives.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Mitigation:} Diverse training data, bias evaluation benchmarks, human review of outputs, red teaming.

\subsection{Safety and Alignment}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Hallucination:} Models generate confident-sounding but false information. Critical risk in medical, legal, and financial applications.
    \item \textbf{Harmful content:} Models can be manipulated (``jailbroken'') to produce harmful, illegal, or dangerous content.
    \item \textbf{Privacy:} Models may memorise and regurgitate training data, including personal information.
    \item \textbf{Alignment:} Ensuring models behave as intended and follow human values, even in edge cases.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Responsible AI Principles}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Transparency:} Users should know when they are interacting with AI.
    \item \textbf{Fairness:} Systems should not discriminate based on protected characteristics.
    \item \textbf{Privacy:} Handle personal data responsibly. Comply with GDPR and data protection laws.
    \item \textbf{Safety:} Implement guardrails, content filters, and output validation.
    \item \textbf{Human oversight:} For critical decisions, AI should assist humans, not replace them.
    \item \textbf{Accountability:} There should always be a human responsible for AI system outputs.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{cautionbox}
In your RevSync project, ECU firmware modifications can affect vehicle safety. Any AI system that recommends or generates tune files \textbf{must} have rigorous verification to ensure tunes are safe and will not damage engines or compromise vehicle control. This is not a theoretical concern---it has real-world physical consequences.
\end{cautionbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand the Transformer architecture: self-attention (queries, keys, values), multi-head attention, positional encoding, encoder vs.\,decoder, BERT vs.\,GPT, training approaches (pre-training, fine-tuning, RLHF), prompting vs.\,fine-tuning trade-offs, model scale, and AI ethics. This is the foundation for understanding and working with all modern AI systems.}



% ============================================================
\chapter{LLM Integration + RAG + Verification}

\section{What Are LLMs?}

\textbf{Large Language Models (LLMs)} are neural networks trained on massive amounts of text data that can understand and generate human language. They are the technology behind ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, and similar AI assistants.

\textbf{Key characteristics:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Scale:} Billions of parameters trained on trillions of tokens of text.
    \item \textbf{Emergent abilities:} At sufficient scale, capabilities ``emerge'' that were not explicitly trained: reasoning, coding, translation, summarisation.
    \item \textbf{Few-shot learning:} Can perform new tasks from just a few examples in the prompt, without retraining.
    \item \textbf{Foundation models:} Pre-trained on general data, then fine-tuned or prompted for specific tasks.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Major LLM families:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{GPT (OpenAI):} GPT-3.5, GPT-4, GPT-4o. Generative, decoder-only architecture. Best for text generation.
    \item \textbf{Claude (Anthropic):} Claude 3 Opus/Sonnet/Haiku. Focus on safety and helpfulness.
    \item \textbf{Gemini (Google):} Multimodal (text + images). Integrated into Google products.
    \item \textbf{Llama (Meta):} Open-source. Llama 2, Llama 3. Can self-host.
    \item \textbf{Mistral:} Open-source, efficient. Good performance/size ratio.
\end{itemize}

\section{Transformer Foundations}

Modern LLMs are built on the \textbf{transformer architecture}. If you understand the transformer at a high level, prompt engineering and RAG stop feeling like magic and start feeling like engineering.

\subsection{Why Transformers Replaced Older Sequence Models}

Before transformers, NLP relied heavily on RNNs and LSTMs. Those models processed text one token at a time. That created two big problems:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item long-range context was hard to preserve
    \item training was slow because sequence steps could not be processed fully in parallel
\end{itemize}

Transformers solved both by using \textbf{self-attention}: each token can look at other tokens directly.

\begin{intuitionbox}
Think of self-attention as every word in a sentence asking, ``Which other words matter most for understanding me right now?'' Instead of remembering the whole sentence through a narrow hidden state, the model can selectively look across the sequence.
\end{intuitionbox}

\subsection{Queries, Keys, and Values}

Self-attention is often explained using three vectors for each token:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Query (Q):} what this token is looking for
    \item \textbf{Key (K):} what this token offers as a match target
    \item \textbf{Value (V):} the information this token contributes if it is attended to
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Plain-English example:} in the sentence ``The rider flashed the tune because \underline{it} improved throttle response,'' the word ``it'' should pay strong attention to ``tune'', not ``rider''. Attention helps the model decide that.

\subsection{Positional Encoding}

Attention by itself does not know token order. The model therefore needs \textbf{positional information} so it can distinguish:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item ``dog bites man''
    \item ``man bites dog''
\end{itemize}

These use the same words but clearly do not mean the same thing.

\subsection{Encoder vs Decoder}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Encoder models} read the whole input and build a rich representation. BERT is the classic example.
    \item \textbf{Decoder models} generate the next token one step at a time. GPT is the classic example.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Why this matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item encoder-style models are strong for understanding and classification
    \item decoder-style models are strong for generation and open-ended response
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Masked Attention}

GPT-style models use \textbf{masked attention} during training and inference so a token can only attend to earlier tokens, not future ones. This is what makes next-token prediction coherent.

\subsection{BERT vs GPT}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Property} & \textbf{BERT} & \textbf{GPT} \\
\midrule
Architecture & Encoder & Decoder \\
Primary strength & Understanding & Generation \\
Reads context & Bidirectionally & Left-to-right \\
Common use & Classification, NER, search & Chat, coding, summarisation \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\subsection{Training vs Inference}

\textbf{Training:} the model learns parameters from data.

\textbf{Inference:} the trained model is used to generate predictions or text for real users.

\textbf{Why this distinction matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item training is compute-heavy and expensive
    \item inference must often be low-latency and cost-controlled
    \item many production AI problems are inference-system problems, not model-training problems
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Prompting vs Fine-Tuning}

\textbf{Prompting} means steering a general model through instructions and examples.

\textbf{Fine-tuning} means changing the model's weights on task-specific data.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Approach} & \textbf{Strength} & \textbf{Weakness} \\
\midrule
Prompting & Fast, flexible, cheap to iterate & Less consistent on narrow tasks \\
Fine-tuning & More task-specific behaviour & Higher cost and more operational complexity \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked ``Why are transformers important?'', do not answer vaguely with ``because they are powerful.'' Say that self-attention improves long-range context handling and parallel training, which made modern large language models practical at scale.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Prompt Engineering}

\textbf{Prompt engineering} is the art and science of writing instructions (prompts) that make LLMs produce the best possible output. This is a critical skill for AI engineers.

\subsection{Prompt Patterns}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Prompt engineering patterns}]
import openai

client = openai.OpenAI()  # uses OPENAI_API_KEY env var

# ---- ZERO-SHOT: Just ask the question ----
response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="gpt-4",
    messages=[{
        "role": "user",
        "content": "Classify this review as positive or negative: "
                   "'The motorcycle tune improved my horsepower "
                   "by 15%. Amazing results!'"
    }]
)
# Output: "Positive"

# ---- FEW-SHOT: Provide examples first ----
response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="gpt-4",
    messages=[
        {"role": "system",
         "content": "Classify motorcycle tune reviews."},
        {"role": "user",
         "content": "Review: 'Engine runs smoother now'\nClass:"},
        {"role": "assistant", "content": "Positive"},
        {"role": "user",
         "content": "Review: 'Bike stalled after flashing'\nClass:"},
        {"role": "assistant", "content": "Negative"},
        {"role": "user",
         "content": "Review: 'Added 20hp, handles great'\nClass:"},
    ]
)
# Output: "Positive" (learned the pattern from examples)

# ---- CHAIN-OF-THOUGHT: Ask it to think step by step ----
response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="gpt-4",
    messages=[{
        "role": "user",
        "content": """A motorcycle ECU tune costs $250.
The shop offers a 15% discount for first-time customers
and a further 5% for paying in cash.
What is the final price? Think step by step."""
    }]
)
# Output explains each step: 250 * 0.85 = 212.50, then
#        212.50 * 0.95 = 201.875 ~ $201.88

# ---- SYSTEM PROMPT: Set behaviour and constraints ----
response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="gpt-4",
    messages=[
        {"role": "system",
         "content": """You are a motorcycle ECU tuning expert.
Rules:
- Only answer questions about motorcycle tuning.
- Always cite safety warnings.
- Never provide tunes for emissions-controlled vehicles.
- Respond in JSON format with keys: answer, safety_notes."""},
        {"role": "user",
         "content": "What RPM should I set for idle on a Yamaha MT-07?"}
    ]
)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Structured Output}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Getting structured JSON output from LLMs}]
import json

response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="gpt-4",
    messages=[
        {"role": "system",
         "content": """Extract structured data from the text.
Return valid JSON with these fields:
- name: person's full name
- skills: list of technical skills
- experience_years: integer
- location: city/country"""},
        {"role": "user",
         "content": "Amzat Karim is a software engineer in London "
                    "with 3 years of experience in Python, Django, "
                    "React Native, and machine learning."}
    ],
    response_format={"type": "json_object"}  # enforce JSON output
)

data = json.loads(response.choices[0].message.content)
print(data)
# {"name": "Amzat Karim", "skills": ["Python", "Django", ...],
#  "experience_years": 3, "location": "London"}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{What Is RAG?}

\textbf{RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation)} is a technique that improves LLM responses by first \emph{retrieving} relevant information from a knowledge base, then \emph{generating} an answer grounded in that information.

\textbf{Why RAG?} LLMs have limitations:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Knowledge cutoff:} LLMs only know what was in their training data. They do not know about recent events or your private data.
    \item \textbf{Hallucination:} LLMs sometimes generate plausible-sounding but incorrect information.
    \item \textbf{No access to private data:} They cannot access your company's documents, databases, or proprietary information.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{RAG solves these by:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Indexing:} Convert your documents into embeddings and store in a vector database.
    \item \textbf{Retrieval:} When a user asks a question, find the most relevant document chunks.
    \item \textbf{Generation:} Feed the retrieved chunks + user question to the LLM, which generates an answer grounded in your data.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{RAG Pipeline Diagram}

\begin{verbatim}
User Question
     |
     v
[Embedding Model] --> query vector
     |
     v
[Vector Database] --> retrieve top-k similar chunks
     |
     v
[LLM] <-- user question + retrieved context
     |
     v
Grounded Answer (with citations)
\end{verbatim}

\section{Embeddings and Vector Databases}

\textbf{Embeddings} are dense numerical vectors that represent the \emph{meaning} of text. Similar meanings produce similar vectors.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Creating embeddings and using vector search}]
# ---- CREATING EMBEDDINGS ----
# Option 1: OpenAI embeddings
response = client.embeddings.create(
    model="text-embedding-3-small",
    input="motorcycle ECU tuning performance"
)
vector = response.data[0].embedding  # list of 1536 floats

# Option 2: Local embeddings with sentence-transformers
# pip install sentence-transformers
from sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer

embed_model = SentenceTransformer("all-MiniLM-L6-v2")
vectors = embed_model.encode([
    "motorcycle ECU tuning",
    "engine performance modification",
    "cooking pasta recipes"
])
# vectors[0] and vectors[1] will be close (similar meaning)
# vectors[2] will be far from both (different topic)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Vector Databases}

\textbf{Vector databases} are specialised databases for storing, indexing, and searching embeddings efficiently:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{FAISS (Facebook):} Open-source, runs locally. Fast for millions of vectors. No persistence by default (in-memory).
    \item \textbf{ChromaDB:} Open-source, easy to use, built for LLM applications. Runs locally or as a server.
    \item \textbf{Pinecone:} Managed cloud service. Zero infrastructure. Scales automatically.
    \item \textbf{Weaviate:} Open-source, supports hybrid search (vector + keyword).
    \item \textbf{pgvector:} PostgreSQL extension for vector storage. Use your existing Postgres database.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={RAG with ChromaDB}]
# pip install chromadb
import chromadb

# 1. Create a collection (like a table)
client = chromadb.Client()
collection = client.create_collection("docs")

# 2. Add documents (automatically creates embeddings)
collection.add(
    documents=[
        "ECU tuning increases engine horsepower by modifying "
        "fuel injection and ignition timing maps.",
        "RevSync supports Yamaha MT-07, Kawasaki Z650, "
        "and Honda CB650R motorcycles.",
        "Always back up the stock ECU map before flashing "
        "a custom tune to avoid bricking the ECU.",
    ],
    ids=["doc1", "doc2", "doc3"],
    metadatas=[
        {"source": "tuning_guide"},
        {"source": "compatibility"},
        {"source": "safety"},
    ]
)

# 3. Query (find relevant documents)
results = collection.query(
    query_texts=["Which bikes does RevSync support?"],
    n_results=2  # return top 2 most relevant
)
print(results["documents"])
# [['RevSync supports Yamaha MT-07, Kawasaki Z650...',
#   'ECU tuning increases engine horsepower...']]
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Document Chunking Strategies}

\textbf{Chunking} is how you split documents before embedding them. This is critical---bad chunking ruins retrieval quality.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Fixed-size chunks:} Split every $N$ characters/tokens. Simple but may cut sentences mid-thought.
    \item \textbf{Sentence-based:} Split on sentence boundaries. Preserves semantic units.
    \item \textbf{Paragraph-based:} Each paragraph is a chunk. Good for well-structured documents.
    \item \textbf{Recursive character splitting:} Try to split on paragraphs first, then sentences, then words. The LangChain default.
    \item \textbf{Semantic chunking:} Use embeddings to detect topic boundaries. Most sophisticated.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Document chunking}]
# Using LangChain's text splitter
# pip install langchain
from langchain.text_splitter import RecursiveCharacterTextSplitter

splitter = RecursiveCharacterTextSplitter(
    chunk_size=500,       # max characters per chunk
    chunk_overlap=50,     # overlap between chunks (context bridge)
    separators=["\n\n", "\n", ". ", " ", ""]  # try these in order
)

text = open("motorcycle_tuning_guide.txt").read()
chunks = splitter.split_text(text)
print(f"Split into {len(chunks)} chunks")
for i, chunk in enumerate(chunks[:3]):
    print(f"\nChunk {i}: ({len(chunk)} chars)")
    print(chunk[:100] + "...")
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
\textbf{Chunk size guidelines:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item 200--500 tokens: Good for precise Q\&A retrieval.
    \item 500--1000 tokens: Good for summarisation and broader context.
    \item Add 10--20\% overlap to avoid losing context at chunk boundaries.
\end{itemize}
\end{tipbox}

\section{Complete RAG Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Production RAG pipeline}]
import chromadb
import openai

client_ai = openai.OpenAI()
client_db = chromadb.Client()

# 1. Index documents
collection = client_db.create_collection("knowledge_base")

documents = [
    "ECU stands for Engine Control Unit. It is the computer "
    "that controls engine operation including fuel injection, "
    "ignition timing, and emission controls.",
    "Flashing a tune involves writing new calibration data "
    "to the ECU's memory. This modifies engine behaviour.",
    "Always perform a health check before flashing. Verify "
    "battery voltage is above 12.5V and engine is cool.",
    # ... hundreds more chunks from your knowledge base
]

collection.add(
    documents=documents,
    ids=[f"doc_{i}" for i in range(len(documents))]
)

# 2. RAG query function
def rag_query(question, n_results=3):
    # Step 1: Retrieve relevant chunks
    results = collection.query(
        query_texts=[question],
        n_results=n_results
    )
    context = "\n\n".join(results["documents"][0])

    # Step 2: Generate answer using retrieved context
    response = client_ai.chat.completions.create(
        model="gpt-4",
        messages=[
            {"role": "system",
             "content": f"""Answer the user's question using ONLY
the provided context. If the context doesn't contain enough
information, say "I don't have enough information to answer that."
Always cite which part of the context supports your answer.

Context:
{context}"""},
            {"role": "user", "content": question}
        ],
        temperature=0.1  # low temperature for factual answers
    )
    return response.choices[0].message.content

# 3. Use it
answer = rag_query("What should I check before flashing an ECU?")
print(answer)
# "Before flashing an ECU, you should perform a health check.
#  Specifically, verify that the battery voltage is above 12.5V
#  and that the engine is cool. [Source: doc_2]"
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Verification and Hallucination Detection}

\textbf{Hallucination} is when an LLM generates information that sounds correct but is fabricated. This is a critical problem in production AI systems.

\textbf{Your VeriRAG project} addresses this with a \textbf{verify-repair loop}:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Claim decomposition:} Break the LLM's response into individual factual claims.
    \item \textbf{Evidence retrieval:} For each claim, find supporting evidence in the knowledge base.
    \item \textbf{Verification:} Check if each claim is supported, contradicted, or unverifiable.
    \item \textbf{Repair:} Replace or remove unsupported claims. Regenerate if needed.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Claim verification pattern (VeriRAG concept)}]
def verify_response(response_text, knowledge_base):
    # 1. Decompose into claims
    claims = decompose_claims(response_text)

    verified_claims = []
    for claim in claims:
        # 2. Find supporting evidence
        evidence = knowledge_base.search(claim, top_k=3)

        # 3. Verify claim against evidence
        verification = verify_claim(claim, evidence)

        if verification.supported:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "SUPPORTED",
                "evidence": verification.evidence_text,
                "confidence": verification.score
            })
        elif verification.contradicted:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "CONTRADICTED",
                "correction": verification.correct_info
            })
        else:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "UNVERIFIABLE",
                "note": "No evidence found in knowledge base"
            })

    return verified_claims
\end{lstlisting}

\section{RAG Evaluation Metrics}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Faithfulness:} Is the generated answer factually consistent with the retrieved context? Does it only say things supported by the sources?
    \item \textbf{Relevance (Answer):} Does the answer actually address the user's question?
    \item \textbf{Relevance (Context):} Are the retrieved chunks actually relevant to the question?
    \item \textbf{Groundedness:} Is every claim in the answer traceable to a specific source?
    \item \textbf{Recall:} Did the retrieval step find all relevant documents?
    \item \textbf{Precision:} Were the retrieved documents actually relevant (not noise)?
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={RAG evaluation with RAGAS}]
# pip install ragas
from ragas import evaluate
from ragas.metrics import (
    faithfulness, answer_relevancy,
    context_precision, context_recall
)

# Prepare evaluation data
eval_data = {
    "question": ["What is ECU tuning?"],
    "answer": ["ECU tuning modifies engine parameters..."],
    "contexts": [["ECU stands for Engine Control Unit..."]],
    "ground_truth": ["ECU tuning modifies fuel injection..."
                     "and ignition timing maps in the ECU."]
}

result = evaluate(
    eval_data,
    metrics=[faithfulness, answer_relevancy,
             context_precision, context_recall]
)
print(result)
# faithfulness: 0.95  (answer sticks to retrieved facts)
# answer_relevancy: 0.88  (answer addresses the question)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{AI Ethics and Safety}

As an AI engineer, you have a responsibility to build systems that are safe, fair, and transparent:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Bias:} LLMs can reflect and amplify biases present in training data (gender, racial, cultural). Always test for bias in your outputs.
    \item \textbf{Hallucination:} Never deploy LLM responses without verification for high-stakes applications (medical, legal, financial).
    \item \textbf{Privacy:} Do not send sensitive/personal data to external LLM APIs without consent and proper data handling agreements.
    \item \textbf{Transparency:} Users should know when they are interacting with AI. Label AI-generated content.
    \item \textbf{Guardrails:} Implement content filters, output validation, and rate limiting to prevent misuse.
    \item \textbf{Human oversight:} For critical decisions, AI should assist humans, not replace them. Keep a human in the loop.
\end{itemize}

\begin{cautionbox}
In your RevSync project, ECU firmware modifications can affect vehicle safety. An AI system that recommends or generates tune files \textbf{must} have rigorous verification to ensure tunes are safe and will not damage engines or compromise vehicle control.
\end{cautionbox}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{VeriRAG} project is a direct implementation of these concepts: it builds a RAG pipeline with claim-level verification to reduce hallucination. The verify-repair loop ensures that generated responses are grounded in source documents, making it suitable for research and high-stakes applications where accuracy is critical.
\end{projectbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand LLM integration (API calls, prompt engineering patterns, structured output), RAG (retrieval-augmented generation with embeddings, vector databases, and document chunking), verification and hallucination detection, RAG evaluation metrics, and AI ethics. This is directly applicable to your VeriRAG and CareerForge AI projects.}



% CHAPTER 28 — GIT + GITHUB
% ============================================================
\chapter{Git + GitHub}

\section{Core Git Workflow}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Essential Git commands}]
# Setup
git init                      # initialise repository
git clone <url>               # clone remote repository

# Daily workflow
git status                    # check what's changed
git add .                     # stage all changes
git add file.py               # stage specific file
git commit -m "Add user auth" # commit with message
git push origin main          # push to remote

# Branches
git branch feature/auth       # create branch
git checkout feature/auth     # switch to branch
git checkout -b feature/auth  # create AND switch
git merge feature/auth        # merge into current branch
git branch -d feature/auth    # delete branch

# Viewing history
git log --oneline -10         # last 10 commits, compact
git log --graph --oneline     # visual branch graph
git diff                      # unstaged changes
git diff --staged             # staged changes

# Undoing
git stash                     # temporarily save changes
git stash pop                 # restore stashed changes
git reset --soft HEAD~1       # undo last commit, keep changes
git revert <commit>           # create reverse commit

# Pull requests (GitHub workflow)
# 1. Create feature branch
# 2. Make changes and commit
# 3. Push branch to GitHub
# 4. Open Pull Request on GitHub
# 5. Code review by teammates
# 6. Merge PR
# 7. Delete feature branch
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Best Practices}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Write clear, imperative commit messages: ``Add user authentication'' not ``added stuff''.
    \item Commit often, push regularly.
    \item One feature per branch.
    \item Always use pull requests for team projects.
    \item Never commit secrets (passwords, API keys, \texttt{.env} files).
    \item Add a \texttt{.gitignore} file to exclude build artifacts, \texttt{node\_modules}, \texttt{venv}, etc.
\end{enumerate}
