\checkpoint{You now understand Git: commits, branches, merges, pull requests, conflict resolution, and best practices. Git is used in every software project.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 29 — DOCKER + CI/CD + JENKINS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Docker + CI/CD + Jenkins}

\section{Docker}

\textbf{Docker} packages your application and all its dependencies into a \textbf{container}---a lightweight, portable, reproducible environment.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Dockerfile for Django}]
# Dockerfile
FROM python:3.11-slim

WORKDIR /app

# Install dependencies
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Copy application code
COPY . .

# Collect static files
RUN python manage.py collectstatic --noinput

# Expose port
EXPOSE 8000

# Run the application
CMD ["gunicorn", "revsync.wsgi:application",
     "--bind", "0.0.0.0:8000", "--workers", "3"]
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Docker Compose for multi-service setup}]
# docker-compose.yml
version: "3.8"
services:
  db:
    image: postgres:15
    environment:
      POSTGRES_DB: revsync
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD: secret
    volumes:
      - pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data

  backend:
    build: ./backend
    ports:
      - "8000:8000"
    environment:
      DATABASE_URL: postgres://postgres:secret@db:5432/revsync
    depends_on:
      - db

  redis:
    image: redis:7-alpine

volumes:
  pgdata:
\end{lstlisting}

\section{CI/CD}

\textbf{CI (Continuous Integration):} Automatically run tests every time code is pushed.

\textbf{CD (Continuous Delivery/Deployment):} Automatically deploy tested code to production.

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={GitHub Actions CI pipeline}]
# .github/workflows/ci.yml
name: CI Pipeline
on: [push, pull_request]
jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/setup-python@v5
        with:
          python-version: "3.11"
      - run: pip install -r requirements.txt
      - run: python manage.py test
      - run: flake8 .
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Jenkins}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Open-source automation server for CI/CD.
    \item Uses \textbf{Jenkinsfile} (pipeline as code) or UI-based configuration.
    \item Supports \textbf{stages}: Build $\to$ Test $\to$ Deploy.
    \item Integrates with Docker, Git, AWS, Kubernetes.
\end{itemize}

\checkpoint{You now understand Docker (images, containers, Dockerfiles, Compose), CI/CD (GitHub Actions, automated testing/deployment), and Jenkins basics.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 30 — AWS + KUBERNETES BASICS
% ============================================================
\chapter{AWS + Kubernetes Basics}

\section{AWS Core Services}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{EC2:} Virtual servers in the cloud. Run your Django app here.
    \item \textbf{S3:} Object storage (files, images, tune files, backups).
    \item \textbf{RDS:} Managed relational databases (PostgreSQL, MySQL).
    \item \textbf{Lambda:} Serverless functions (run code without managing servers).
    \item \textbf{CloudFront:} CDN (Content Delivery Network) for fast global content delivery.
    \item \textbf{IAM:} Identity and Access Management (users, roles, permissions).
    \item \textbf{VPC:} Virtual Private Cloud (network isolation).
    \item \textbf{ECS/EKS:} Container orchestration (run Docker containers at scale).
\end{itemize}

\section{Kubernetes (K8s) Basics}

Kubernetes automates deployment, scaling, and management of containerised applications.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Pod:} Smallest deployable unit. One or more containers running together.
    \item \textbf{Deployment:} Declarative updates for pods. Ensures desired number of replicas.
    \item \textbf{Service:} Network endpoint that exposes pods (load balancing).
    \item \textbf{ConfigMap:} Non-sensitive configuration data.
    \item \textbf{Secret:} Sensitive configuration data (passwords, tokens).
    \item \textbf{Ingress:} HTTP routing rules (like a reverse proxy).
\end{itemize}

\checkpoint{You now understand AWS core services and Kubernetes concepts. Both are essential for deploying production applications at scale.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 31 — SECURITY FUNDAMENTALS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Security Fundamentals}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} do not rush through the vulnerability list. For each risk, ask four things: what is the attack, why does it work, what damage can it cause, and what specific coding or infrastructure practice prevents it?
\end{studybox}

\section{Security Mindset}

\textbf{The first rule of security:} Never trust user input. Every piece of data that comes from outside your system---form fields, URL parameters, headers, file uploads, API requests---must be treated as potentially malicious.

\textbf{Defence in depth:} Security is not a single wall but multiple layers. If one layer fails, the next should catch the attack. Implement security at every level: input validation, authentication, authorisation, encryption, monitoring.

\begin{intuitionbox}
Security is really the discipline of asking, ``What happens if someone uses this system in the worst possible way?'' Good security engineering does not assume users, networks, or clients behave ideally.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{OWASP Top 10 Vulnerabilities}

The \textbf{OWASP Top 10} is the most widely referenced list of web application security risks. Every developer must know these:

\subsection{1. Injection (SQL Injection, Command Injection)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker inserts malicious code into queries or commands by manipulating user input.

\textbf{Example of vulnerable code:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={SQL Injection: vulnerable vs.\,safe}]
# VULNERABLE -- NEVER DO THIS
query = f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '{email}'"
# If email = "'; DROP TABLE users; --"
# The query becomes: SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '';
#                    DROP TABLE users; --'
# YOUR ENTIRE USERS TABLE IS DELETED.

# SAFE -- Always use parameterised queries
cursor.execute(
    "SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = %s",
    (email,)  # parameter passed separately, NEVER concatenated
)

# SAFE -- Django ORM (always parameterised internally)
User.objects.filter(email=email)

# SAFE -- SQLAlchemy
session.query(User).filter_by(email=email).first()
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{2. Broken Authentication}

\textbf{What it is:} Weak passwords, exposed tokens, session hijacking, no brute-force protection.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Hash passwords with \textbf{bcrypt} or \textbf{argon2} (never MD5 or SHA-256 without salt).
    \item Use short-lived access tokens (15 min) + longer refresh tokens (7 days).
    \item Implement account lockout after failed attempts.
    \item Use multi-factor authentication (MFA) for sensitive operations.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Password hashing with bcrypt}]
# pip install bcrypt
import bcrypt

# Hash a password (during registration)
password = "my_secure_password_123"
salt = bcrypt.gensalt(rounds=12)  # 12 rounds = good balance
hashed = bcrypt.hashpw(password.encode("utf-8"), salt)
# Store 'hashed' in your database. NEVER store plain password.

# Verify a password (during login)
input_password = "my_secure_password_123"
is_valid = bcrypt.checkpw(
    input_password.encode("utf-8"), hashed
)
print(f"Password valid: {is_valid}")  # True
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Password hashing in Node.js}]
const bcrypt = require("bcrypt");

// Hash
const hash = await bcrypt.hash("my_password", 12);

// Verify
const isValid = await bcrypt.compare("my_password", hash);
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{3. Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker injects malicious JavaScript into pages viewed by other users, stealing cookies, sessions, or personal data.

\textbf{Types:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Stored XSS:} Malicious script is saved in the database (e.g., in a comment) and executed when others view it.
    \item \textbf{Reflected XSS:} Malicious script is in a URL parameter and reflected back to the user.
    \item \textbf{DOM-based XSS:} JavaScript on the page processes user input unsafely.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Escape all output.} Django does this automatically in templates. React does this automatically with JSX.
    \item \textbf{Never use} \texttt{dangerouslySetInnerHTML} in React (unless you sanitise first).
    \item \textbf{Set Content Security Policy (CSP) headers} to restrict where scripts can load from.
    \item \textbf{Use HttpOnly cookies} for tokens (JavaScript cannot read them).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={XSS prevention: sanitising user input}]
# pip install bleach
import bleach

def sanitize_html(user_input):
    return bleach.clean(
        user_input,
        tags=["b", "i", "u", "a", "p", "br"],  # allowed tags
        attributes={"a": ["href", "title"]},
        strip=True  # remove disallowed tags entirely
    )

# Example
dirty = '<script>alert("XSS")</script><b>Hello</b>'
clean = sanitize_html(dirty)
print(clean)  # '<b>Hello</b>' -- script tag removed
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{4. Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker tricks a logged-in user into making unintended requests to your server (e.g., transferring money, changing email) by embedding requests in malicious pages.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Use \textbf{CSRF tokens} in forms. Django includes \texttt{\{% csrf\_token \%\}} automatically.
    \item Set \textbf{SameSite=Strict} or \textbf{SameSite=Lax} on cookies.
    \item Verify \textbf{Origin/Referer} headers on state-changing requests.
    \item For APIs: use Bearer tokens instead of cookies (tokens are not sent automatically).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{5. Security Misconfiguration}

\textbf{What it is:} Default credentials, unnecessary features enabled, verbose error messages in production, missing security headers.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Set \textbf{DEBUG = False} in production (Django).
    \item Set \texttt{NODE\_ENV=production} (Node.js).
    \item Remove default admin accounts and change default passwords.
    \item Disable directory listing on web servers.
    \item Return generic error messages to users (log details server-side).
\end{itemize}

\section{JWT (JSON Web Token) Security}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Secure JWT implementation in Django}]
# settings.py -- Django Simple JWT configuration
from datetime import timedelta

SIMPLE_JWT = {
    "ACCESS_TOKEN_LIFETIME": timedelta(minutes=15),  # short-lived!
    "REFRESH_TOKEN_LIFETIME": timedelta(days=7),
    "ROTATE_REFRESH_TOKENS": True,   # new refresh token each time
    "BLACKLIST_AFTER_ROTATION": True, # old refresh token invalidated
    "ALGORITHM": "HS256",
    "SIGNING_KEY": SECRET_KEY,  # use a strong, unique secret
    "AUTH_HEADER_TYPES": ("Bearer",),
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Secure JWT in Node.js}]
const jwt = require("jsonwebtoken");

// Generate tokens
function generateTokens(userId) {
    const accessToken = jwt.sign(
        { sub: userId, type: "access" },
        process.env.JWT_ACCESS_SECRET,
        { expiresIn: "15m" }  // short-lived
    );
    const refreshToken = jwt.sign(
        { sub: userId, type: "refresh" },
        process.env.JWT_REFRESH_SECRET,  // different secret!
        { expiresIn: "7d" }
    );
    return { accessToken, refreshToken };
}

// Verify token middleware
function authMiddleware(req, res, next) {
    const token = req.headers.authorization?.split(" ")[1];
    if (!token) return res.status(401).json({ error: "No token" });
    try {
        const decoded = jwt.verify(token, process.env.JWT_ACCESS_SECRET);
        if (decoded.type !== "access") throw new Error("Wrong token type");
        req.user = decoded;
        next();
    } catch (err) {
        return res.status(401).json({ error: "Invalid or expired token" });
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Secure Headers}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Security headers (Django middleware)}]
# Django: use django-csp and SecurityMiddleware
MIDDLEWARE = [
    "django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware",
    # ...
]

# settings.py
SECURE_BROWSER_XSS_FILTER = True
SECURE_CONTENT_TYPE_NOSNIFF = True
SECURE_SSL_REDIRECT = True  # redirect HTTP to HTTPS
SESSION_COOKIE_SECURE = True  # only send cookies over HTTPS
CSRF_COOKIE_SECURE = True
SESSION_COOKIE_HTTPONLY = True  # JS cannot access session cookie
X_FRAME_OPTIONS = "DENY"  # prevent clickjacking
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Security headers (Express with helmet)}]
// npm install helmet
const helmet = require("helmet");
app.use(helmet());  // sets 11+ security headers automatically

// Includes: X-Content-Type-Options, X-Frame-Options,
// Strict-Transport-Security, Content-Security-Policy, etc.
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Rate Limiting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Rate limiting in Express}]
// npm install express-rate-limit
const rateLimit = require("express-rate-limit");

// General API rate limit
const apiLimiter = rateLimit({
    windowMs: 15 * 60 * 1000, // 15 minutes
    max: 100,                  // 100 requests per window
    message: { error: "Too many requests, try again later" }
});
app.use("/api/", apiLimiter);

// Stricter limit for auth endpoints (prevent brute force)
const authLimiter = rateLimit({
    windowMs: 15 * 60 * 1000,
    max: 5,  // only 5 login attempts per 15 minutes
    message: { error: "Too many login attempts" }
});
app.use("/api/auth/", authLimiter);
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Security Best Practices Checklist}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Hash passwords} with bcrypt/argon2 (never store plain text or use MD5/SHA).
    \item Use \textbf{HTTPS everywhere} (TLS/SSL). Never transmit credentials over HTTP.
    \item Store secrets in \textbf{environment variables}, never in code or version control.
    \item Apply \textbf{least privilege}: give minimum permissions needed.
    \item Validate and sanitise \textbf{all user input} server-side (not just client-side).
    \item Set \textbf{secure cookie flags}: HttpOnly, Secure, SameSite.
    \item Use \textbf{parameterised queries} always (Django ORM does this automatically).
    \item Keep dependencies \textbf{updated} (\texttt{npm audit}, \texttt{pip-audit}, Dependabot).
    \item Implement \textbf{rate limiting} on all API endpoints.
    \item Log security events for \textbf{auditing} (failed logins, permission denials).
    \item Use \textbf{CORS} properly---never set \texttt{Access-Control-Allow-Origin: *} in production.
    \item \textbf{Rotate secrets} regularly (API keys, JWT secrets, database passwords).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{cautionbox}
Security is not optional. A single vulnerability can expose all your users' data. Your RevSync project handles ECU firmware---a security breach could have real-world physical consequences. Treat every line of security code as critical.
\end{cautionbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Security starts with a mindset: distrust inputs, limit permissions, and assume failure paths matter.
    \item OWASP vulnerabilities are practical engineering problems, not abstract theory.
    \item Parameterized queries, escaping, CSRF protection, secure headers, rate limiting, and secret management are everyday controls.
    \item A secure system needs multiple layers so that one missed check does not become a total compromise.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why does SQL injection happen?
    \item What is the practical difference between XSS and CSRF?
    \item Why should refresh tokens and access tokens have different lifetimes?
    \item What does ``defence in depth'' mean in a real backend?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand security deeply: OWASP Top 10 vulnerabilities (SQL injection, XSS, CSRF, broken auth, misconfiguration), password hashing with bcrypt, JWT security, secure headers, rate limiting, CORS, and security best practices. Apply these to every project you build.}


\section{Chapter Review: Security}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Never trust user input.} Validate, sanitise, and escape everything.
    \item SQL injection is prevented by \textbf{parameterised queries} (never concatenate user input into SQL).
    \item XSS is prevented by \textbf{escaping output} (React and Django do this automatically).
    \item Always hash passwords with \textbf{bcrypt} or \textbf{argon2}.
    \item Use \textbf{short-lived access tokens} (15 min) + \textbf{refresh tokens} (7 days) for JWT auth.
    \item Set \textbf{secure headers} on every response (use helmet in Express, SecurityMiddleware in Django).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Quick Recall Questions}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between authentication and authorisation?
    \item Write a parameterised SQL query that prevents SQL injection.
    \item What are the three types of XSS? How do you prevent each?
    \item Why should you never use MD5 or SHA-256 alone for password hashing?
    \item What is CSRF and how do CSRF tokens prevent it?
    \item What does the HttpOnly cookie flag do?
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 32 — TESTING + DEBUGGING + DOCUMENTATION
% ============================================================
\chapter{Testing + Debugging + Documentation}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} treat it as a day-to-day engineering workflow chapter. These ideas matter most when code stops being a toy and starts being something other people rely on.
\end{studybox}

\section{Why Testing Matters}

\textbf{Testing} is how you prove your code works correctly and continues working as you make changes. Without tests, every code change is a gamble---you might break something without knowing.

\textbf{Types of tests (the testing pyramid):}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Unit tests (base of pyramid):} Test individual functions/methods in isolation. Fast, numerous, cheap. Should be the majority of your tests.
    \item \textbf{Integration tests (middle):} Test how components work together (e.g., API endpoint + database + authentication). Slower, fewer.
    \item \textbf{End-to-end (E2E) tests (top):} Test complete user workflows (e.g., login $\to$ browse $\to$ purchase). Slowest, fewest, most fragile.
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{The testing pyramid rule:} Write many unit tests, some integration tests, and few E2E tests. If E2E tests are the majority, your test suite will be slow and fragile.
\end{notebox}

\section{Testing in Python (pytest)}

\textbf{pytest} is the standard Python testing framework. It is simpler and more powerful than the built-in \texttt{unittest}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={pytest essentials}]
# test_calculator.py
import pytest

# ---- BASIC TESTS ----
def test_addition():
    assert 1 + 1 == 2

def test_string_upper():
    assert "hello".upper() == "HELLO"

def test_list_contains():
    fruits = ["apple", "banana", "cherry"]
    assert "banana" in fruits

# ---- TESTING FOR EXCEPTIONS ----
def test_division_by_zero():
    with pytest.raises(ZeroDivisionError):
        1 / 0

def test_invalid_input():
    with pytest.raises(ValueError, match="negative"):
        withdraw(-100)

# ---- FIXTURES (reusable test setup) ----
@pytest.fixture
def sample_user():
    return {"name": "Amzat", "email": "amzat@example.com", "age": 24}

@pytest.fixture
def db_connection():
    conn = create_connection()
    yield conn  # test runs here
    conn.close()  # cleanup after test

def test_user_name(sample_user):
    assert sample_user["name"] == "Amzat"

def test_user_email(sample_user):
    assert "@" in sample_user["email"]

# ---- PARAMETERISED TESTS (test multiple inputs) ----
@pytest.mark.parametrize("input,expected", [
    (2, 4),
    (3, 9),
    (4, 16),
    (-1, 1),
    (0, 0),
])
def test_square(input, expected):
    assert input ** 2 == expected
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Running pytest}]
# Run all tests
pytest

# Run with verbose output
pytest -v

# Run specific file
pytest test_calculator.py

# Run specific test
pytest test_calculator.py::test_addition

# Run with coverage report
pytest --cov=myapp --cov-report=html

# Run only tests marked as "slow"
pytest -m slow

# Stop on first failure
pytest -x
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Testing Django APIs}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Django REST Framework testing}]
from rest_framework.test import APITestCase, APIClient
from rest_framework import status
from django.contrib.auth import get_user_model

User = get_user_model()

class ProductAPITest(APITestCase):
    def setUp(self):
        # Runs before every test method
        self.user = User.objects.create_user(
            username="testuser", password="testpass123"
        )
        self.client = APIClient()
        self.client.force_authenticate(user=self.user)

    def test_list_products(self):
        response = self.client.get("/api/products/")
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_200_OK)
        self.assertIsInstance(response.data, list)

    def test_create_product(self):
        data = {
            "title": "Test Tune",
            "price": "99.99",
            "category": "electronics"
        }
        response = self.client.post("/api/products/", data)
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_201_CREATED)
        self.assertEqual(response.data["title"], "Test Tune")

    def test_create_product_unauthenticated(self):
        self.client.force_authenticate(user=None)
        response = self.client.post("/api/products/", {"title": "Test"})
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED)

    def test_update_own_product(self):
        # Create first
        create_resp = self.client.post("/api/products/", {
            "title": "Old Title", "price": "50.00",
            "category": "electronics"
        })
        uuid = create_resp.data["uuid"]

        # Update
        response = self.client.patch(
            f"/api/products/{uuid}/",
            {"title": "New Title"}
        )
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_200_OK)
        self.assertEqual(response.data["title"], "New Title")

    def tearDown(self):
        # Runs after every test method (cleanup)
        pass
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Testing in JavaScript (Jest)}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Jest testing for Node.js}]
// calculator.test.js
const { add, divide, fetchUser } = require("./calculator");

// ---- BASIC TESTS ----
describe("Calculator", () => {
    test("adds two numbers", () => {
        expect(add(2, 3)).toBe(5);
    });

    test("handles negative numbers", () => {
        expect(add(-1, -2)).toBe(-3);
    });

    test("throws on division by zero", () => {
        expect(() => divide(10, 0)).toThrow("Cannot divide by zero");
    });
});

// ---- ASYNC TESTS ----
describe("User API", () => {
    test("fetches user by ID", async () => {
        const user = await fetchUser(1);
        expect(user).toHaveProperty("name");
        expect(user.name).toBeDefined();
    });
});

// ---- MOCKING ----
const axios = require("axios");
jest.mock("axios");  // mock the entire module

test("fetches users from API", async () => {
    // Setup: define what the mock should return
    axios.get.mockResolvedValue({
        data: [{ id: 1, name: "Amzat" }]
    });

    const users = await getUsers();
    expect(users).toHaveLength(1);
    expect(users[0].name).toBe("Amzat");
    expect(axios.get).toHaveBeenCalledWith("/api/users");
});
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Mocking}

\textbf{Mocking} replaces real dependencies (databases, APIs, external services) with controlled substitutes during testing. This makes tests fast, deterministic, and independent of external systems.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Mocking in Python}]
from unittest.mock import patch, MagicMock

# Mock an external API call
@patch("myapp.services.requests.get")
def test_fetch_weather(mock_get):
    # Setup mock response
    mock_get.return_value.status_code = 200
    mock_get.return_value.json.return_value = {
        "temperature": 20, "condition": "sunny"
    }

    # Call the function that uses requests.get
    result = get_weather("London")

    assert result["temperature"] == 20
    mock_get.assert_called_once_with(
        "https://api.weather.com/London"
    )
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Test-Driven Development (TDD)}

\textbf{TDD} is a development approach where you write tests \emph{before} writing the code:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Red:} Write a failing test that defines the desired behaviour.
    \item \textbf{Green:} Write the minimum code to make the test pass.
    \item \textbf{Refactor:} Clean up the code while keeping tests green.
    \item Repeat.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{tipbox}
TDD forces you to think about requirements and edge cases \emph{before} coding. It produces better-designed, more testable code. Not every scenario needs TDD, but it is excellent for complex logic, algorithms, and critical business rules.
\end{tipbox}

\section{Debugging Strategy}

When something breaks, follow this systematic approach:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Reproduce} the bug consistently. If you cannot reproduce it, you cannot fix it.
    \item \textbf{Read the error message} and stack trace carefully. It usually tells you exactly what went wrong and where.
    \item \textbf{Isolate} the problem. Which file? Which function? Which line?
    \item \textbf{Check assumptions.} Is the data what you think it is? Print/log variables.
    \item \textbf{Use the debugger.} Set breakpoints, step through code line by line.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Python: \texttt{import pdb; pdb.set\_trace()} or VS Code breakpoints.
        \item JavaScript: \texttt{debugger;} statement or Chrome DevTools.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Binary search.} If the bug is in a large block of code, comment out half and see if the bug persists. Repeat.
    \item \textbf{Check recent changes.} Use \texttt{git diff} or \texttt{git log} to see what changed since it last worked.
    \item \textbf{Fix the root cause}, not the symptom. A quick hack will come back to haunt you.
    \item \textbf{Write a regression test} to prevent the bug from recurring.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Documentation Best Practices}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{README:} Every project must have one. Include: what the project does, how to set it up, how to run it, architecture overview, tech stack, screenshots.
    \item \textbf{API documentation:} Document every endpoint with method, URL, request/response format, auth requirements, error codes. Use Swagger/OpenAPI or DRF's built-in auto-docs.
    \item \textbf{Code comments:} Explain \emph{why}, not \emph{what}. The code itself shows what it does---comments should explain non-obvious reasoning.
    \item \textbf{Docstrings:} Every public function and class should have a docstring explaining purpose, parameters, return values, and exceptions.
    \item \textbf{Architecture Decision Records (ADRs):} Document important technical decisions and their rationale.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Good vs.\,bad comments}]
# BAD: states the obvious
x = x + 1  # increment x by 1

# GOOD: explains WHY
x = x + 1  # compensate for 0-based indexing in the API response

# BAD: no docstring
def process(data):
    ...

# GOOD: clear docstring
def process_tune_file(file_path: str) -> dict:
    """Parse and validate an ECU tune file.

    Args:
        file_path: Absolute path to the .bin tune file.

    Returns:
        Dictionary with keys: checksum, maps, metadata.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the file format is invalid.
        FileNotFoundError: If the file does not exist.
    """
    ...
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Testing gives you confidence to change code safely.
    \item Debugging is a structured investigation, not random trial and error.
    \item Documentation reduces repeated confusion and makes teams faster.
    \item Strong engineers do not just write features; they make those features observable, testable, and understandable.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why does the testing pyramid place unit tests at the base?
    \item What makes a test deterministic?
    \item Why is reproducing a bug the first step in debugging?
    \item What belongs in a README, and what belongs in API documentation?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand testing deeply: the testing pyramid, pytest (fixtures, parametrize, exceptions), Django API testing, Jest for JavaScript, mocking, TDD workflow, systematic debugging strategy, and documentation best practices. Apply testing to every project you build.}


\section{Chapter Review: Testing}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The \textbf{test pyramid}: many unit tests, some integration tests, few E2E tests.
    \item \textbf{pytest} is the standard Python test framework. Use fixtures for reusable setup.
    \item \textbf{Mocking} replaces real dependencies with controlled substitutes.
    \item \textbf{TDD} = write test first, then code, then refactor.
    \item \textbf{Debugging} is a systematic process, not random guessing.
    \item Comments should explain \emph{why}, not \emph{what}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Practical Exercises}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Write unit tests for a calculator module with pytest, including edge cases.
    \item Write an integration test for a Django REST API endpoint.
    \item Practice mocking: test a function that calls an external weather API.
    \item Use TDD to build a password validator (min length, uppercase, number, special char).
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 33 — SYSTEM DESIGN + ARCHITECTURE
% ============================================================
\chapter{System Design + Architecture}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} read system design as a decision-making framework. Do not try to memorise diagrams. Instead, practice asking the right questions in the right order and explaining trade-offs calmly.
\end{studybox}

\section{What Is System Design?}

\textbf{System design} is the process of defining the architecture, components, data flow, and infrastructure of a software system to meet specific requirements. It is how you go from ``I need a motorcycle tuning marketplace'' to a detailed blueprint showing every server, database, cache, and queue.

\textbf{Why it matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A poorly designed system collapses under load, costs too much, or becomes unmaintainable.
    \item System design is tested in senior engineering interviews at every major tech company.
    \item Understanding architecture helps you make better decisions even in day-to-day coding.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
System design is the art of choosing what to optimize first. Every useful architecture is a set of trade-offs between simplicity, latency, consistency, throughput, cost, and operational complexity.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{System Design Process}

Follow this structured approach for any system design problem:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Clarify requirements:}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Functional:} What does it do? (list products, process payments, flash ECU tunes)
        \item \textbf{Non-functional:} How well? (latency $< 200$ms, 99.9\% uptime, handle 10K users)
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Estimate scale:} How many users? Requests/second? Data volume? Read-heavy or write-heavy?
    \item \textbf{Define API:} What endpoints exist? What data flows between components?
    \item \textbf{Design data model:} Database schema, relationships, indexing strategy.
    \item \textbf{High-level architecture:} Draw boxes and arrows showing all major components.
    \item \textbf{Deep dive:} Zoom into critical components (how does the payment system work? how does search scale?).
    \item \textbf{Identify bottlenecks:} Where will the system break under 10x load?
    \item \textbf{Discuss trade-offs:} Every decision has pros and cons. Articulate them.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Key Architectural Concepts}

\subsection{Scaling Strategies}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Vertical scaling (scale up):} Add more power to one machine---more CPU, RAM, faster SSD. Simple but has physical limits. Expensive at the top end.
    \item \textbf{Horizontal scaling (scale out):} Add more machines behind a load balancer. Virtually unlimited scaling. Requires stateless application design.
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
Most modern systems use horizontal scaling. This means your application must be \textbf{stateless}---do not store session data in memory on one server. Use Redis, a database, or JWTs instead.
\end{notebox}

\subsection{Load Balancing}

A \textbf{load balancer} distributes incoming requests across multiple servers:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Round-robin:} Distributes requests evenly in order (Server 1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3...).
    \item \textbf{Least connections:} Sends to the server with fewest active connections.
    \item \textbf{IP hash:} Routes based on client IP (session affinity).
    \item \textbf{Tools:} Nginx, HAProxy, AWS ALB (Application Load Balancer), AWS NLB.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Caching}

\textbf{Caching} stores frequently accessed data in fast storage (memory) to avoid expensive recomputation or database queries:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Client-side cache:} Browser cache, CDN cache. Reduces latency by serving from nearby edge servers.
    \item \textbf{Application cache:} Redis, Memcached. Store query results, session data, computed values.
    \item \textbf{Database cache:} Query cache, materialised views.
    \item \textbf{CDN (Content Delivery Network):} Caches static assets (images, CSS, JS) on servers worldwide. CloudFront, Cloudflare. Users get served from the nearest edge location.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Cache invalidation strategies:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{TTL (Time To Live):} Cache expires after a set time (e.g., 5 minutes).
    \item \textbf{Write-through:} Update cache when you update the database.
    \item \textbf{Cache-aside (lazy loading):} Check cache first; on miss, read from DB and populate cache.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
``There are only two hard things in Computer Science: cache invalidation and naming things.'' --- Phil Karlton. Cache bugs are notoriously difficult to debug because the system works \emph{most of the time}.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Database Design at Scale}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Replication:} Master database handles writes; read replicas handle reads. Improves read throughput.
    \item \textbf{Sharding (partitioning):} Split data across multiple databases by a shard key (e.g., user ID). Each shard holds a subset of data. Enables near-unlimited storage and write throughput.
    \item \textbf{SQL vs.\,NoSQL:}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{SQL (PostgreSQL, MySQL):} Structured data, strong consistency, complex queries, transactions.
        \item \textbf{NoSQL (MongoDB, DynamoDB, Cassandra):} Flexible schema, horizontal scaling, eventual consistency. Good for: logs, analytics, real-time data, document storage.
    \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Message Queues}

\textbf{Message queues} enable asynchronous processing---the producer sends a message and continues immediately; the consumer processes it later:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Use cases:} Background jobs (sending emails, processing uploads, generating reports), decoupling services, handling traffic spikes.
    \item \textbf{Tools:} RabbitMQ, Apache Kafka, AWS SQS, Redis (as a simple queue), Celery (Python task queue).
    \item \textbf{Pattern:} Producer $\to$ Queue $\to$ Consumer. Multiple consumers can process in parallel.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{CAP Theorem}

In a \textbf{distributed system}, you can only guarantee two of three properties:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Consistency (C):} Every read receives the most recent write. All nodes see the same data.
    \item \textbf{Availability (A):} Every request receives a response (success or failure), even if some nodes are down.
    \item \textbf{Partition tolerance (P):} The system continues operating even if network communication between nodes is lost.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{In practice:} Network partitions \emph{will} happen (P is non-negotiable), so you must choose between C and A:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{CP systems} (Consistency + Partition tolerance): Return errors rather than stale data. Example: banking systems, PostgreSQL.
    \item \textbf{AP systems} (Availability + Partition tolerance): Return potentially stale data rather than errors. Example: DNS, Cassandra, DynamoDB (eventual consistency).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Microservices vs.\,Monolith}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Monolith:} Single codebase, single deployment. Simple, fast to start. Your RevSync backend is a monolith (Django).
    \item \textbf{Microservices:} Many small, independent services communicating via APIs. Each can be deployed, scaled, and updated independently.
    \item \textbf{When to use microservices:} When you have separate teams, different scaling needs per component, or need independent deployment. Do not start with microservices---extract them from a monolith when needed.
\end{itemize}

\section{System Design Walkthrough: URL Shortener}

Let us walk through a complete system design example.

\textbf{Problem:} Design a URL shortener (like bit.ly). Users submit a long URL and get a short URL. Visitors to the short URL are redirected to the original.

\textbf{Step 1 --- Requirements:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Functional: Create short URLs, redirect to original, track click count.
    \item Non-functional: Low latency ($< 50$ms redirect), high availability, handle 1000 reads/second.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 2 --- API Design:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{POST /api/shorten} --- Body: \texttt{\{url: "https://long-url.com"\}} $\to$ Response: \texttt{\{short\_url: "abc123"\}}
    \item \texttt{GET /abc123} --- Redirects to original URL (HTTP 301/302).
    \item \texttt{GET /api/stats/abc123} --- Returns click count, referrers.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 3 --- Data Model:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={URL shortener schema}]
CREATE TABLE urls (
    id BIGSERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    short_code VARCHAR(10) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    original_url TEXT NOT NULL,
    created_at TIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW(),
    click_count BIGINT DEFAULT 0,
    expires_at TIMESTAMPTZ
);
CREATE INDEX idx_short_code ON urls(short_code);
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Step 4 --- Architecture:}
\begin{verbatim}
User --> [Load Balancer] --> [App Servers (stateless)]
              |                     |
              v                     v
         [Redis Cache]      [PostgreSQL DB]
         (short_code         (persistent
          -> url mapping)     storage)
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{Step 5 --- Key Decisions:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Short code generation:} Base62 encoding of an auto-incrementing ID. 6 characters = $62^6 = 56$ billion unique URLs.
    \item \textbf{Caching:} Cache popular URLs in Redis. 80\% of traffic goes to 20\% of URLs (Pareto principle).
    \item \textbf{Redirect type:} 301 (permanent) for SEO, 302 (temporary) for tracking clicks.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 6 --- Scaling for 1M users:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Horizontal scaling: multiple app servers behind load balancer.
    \item Database read replicas for stats queries.
    \item Redis cluster for caching.
    \item CDN for the redirect (if possible).
\end{itemize}

\section{System Design: RevSync Architecture}

\begin{verbatim}
[React Native App]
     |
     | HTTPS (JWT Auth)
     v
[Nginx (Load Balancer + SSL)]
     |
     v
[Django + DRF (API Server)]
     |
     +--> [PostgreSQL (Users, Tunes, Orders)]
     |
     +--> [Redis (Caching, Session, Rate Limiting)]
     |
     +--> [S3 (Tune file storage)]
     |
     +--> [Celery Workers (Background jobs:
     |     tune validation, email, notifications)]
     |
     +--> [RabbitMQ/Redis (Message broker)]
     |
     v
[Monitoring: Sentry + Prometheus + Grafana]
\end{verbatim}

\begin{interviewbox}
System design interviews test your ability to: (1) clarify requirements, (2) estimate scale, (3) design APIs, (4) choose the right database, (5) identify bottlenecks, (6) discuss trade-offs. Practice with: design Twitter, design Uber, design a file upload service, design a notification system. Always draw diagrams.
\end{interviewbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Good system design starts with requirements and scale, not with tools.
    \item Caching, queues, load balancing, replication, and sharding solve different classes of problems.
    \item Strong answers explain why a choice was made and what trade-off it introduces.
    \item Interview-quality design is as much about communication and structure as it is about components.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why should you separate functional and non-functional requirements?
    \item What does a load balancer actually solve?
    \item When is replication useful, and when is sharding necessary?
    \item Why is cache invalidation difficult?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand system design deeply: the design process (requirements $\to$ scale $\to$ API $\to$ data model $\to$ architecture $\to$ bottlenecks $\to$ trade-offs), scaling strategies, load balancing, caching (Redis, CDN), database replication and sharding, message queues, CAP theorem, microservices vs.\,monolith, and complete design walkthroughs. You can design systems like URL shorteners, marketplaces, and real-time platforms.}


\section{Chapter Review: System Design}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item System design = requirements $\to$ scale estimation $\to$ API $\to$ data model $\to$ architecture $\to$ bottlenecks $\to$ trade-offs.
    \item \textbf{Horizontal scaling} + \textbf{stateless servers} is the standard approach.
    \item \textbf{Caching} (Redis) and \textbf{CDNs} reduce latency dramatically.
    \item \textbf{CAP theorem}: you must choose between consistency and availability when networks partition.
    \item \textbf{Message queues} enable asynchronous processing and service decoupling.
    \item Start with a \textbf{monolith}; extract microservices only when needed.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Practice Problems}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Design a file upload service (like Dropbox). Consider: storage, CDN, chunked uploads, metadata.
    \item Design a notification system (email, push, SMS). Consider: queues, templates, delivery tracking.
    \item Design RevSync's architecture for 100K users. Where are the bottlenecks?
    \item Explain the CAP theorem with a real-world example.
\end{enumerate}

% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 35 — INTERVIEW + PORTFOLIO PREPARATION
% ============================================================
\chapter{Interview + Portfolio Preparation}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} move from passive reading to active rehearsal. Interview preparation becomes real only when you explain solutions aloud, solve problems under time pressure, and review weak areas honestly.
\end{studybox}

\section{Coding Interview Preparation}

\subsection{The Interview Format}

Most tech company interviews include:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Phone screen} (30--45 min): Basic coding problem, behavioural questions.
    \item \textbf{Technical assessment} (60--90 min): Online coding test (HackerRank, Codility) or take-home project.
    \item \textbf{On-site/virtual loop} (4--6 hours): Multiple rounds testing different skills.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{LeetCode Problem Patterns}

Most coding interview problems fall into these patterns. Learn the pattern, not individual problems:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Two Pointers:} Use two indices moving from opposite ends or same direction. Problems: two sum (sorted array), remove duplicates, container with most water, trapping rain water.

    \item \textbf{Sliding Window:} Maintain a window of elements and slide it across the array. Problems: maximum subarray sum of size $k$, longest substring without repeating characters, minimum window substring.

    \item \textbf{Binary Search:} Search sorted data in $O(\log n)$. Apply to arrays, answer spaces, rotated arrays. Problems: search in rotated sorted array, find peak element, search 2D matrix.

    \item \textbf{BFS/DFS (Graph Traversal):} BFS uses a queue (level-by-level), DFS uses a stack or recursion (go deep). Problems: number of islands, lowest common ancestor, clone graph, course schedule (cycle detection).

    \item \textbf{Dynamic Programming (DP):} Break problem into overlapping subproblems. Build solution bottom-up. Problems: climbing stairs, coin change, longest common subsequence, knapsack, edit distance.

    \item \textbf{Backtracking:} Explore all possibilities by making choices and undoing them. Problems: permutations, combinations, N-Queens, Sudoku solver, word search.

    \item \textbf{Linked List:} Pointer manipulation. Problems: reverse linked list, detect cycle (Floyd's algorithm), merge two sorted lists, remove nth node from end.

    \item \textbf{Stack/Queue:} LIFO/FIFO operations. Problems: valid parentheses, min stack, evaluate reverse Polish notation, daily temperatures.

    \item \textbf{Heap/Priority Queue:} Efficiently find min/max. Problems: K-th largest element, merge K sorted lists, find median from data stream, top K frequent elements.

    \item \textbf{Tree Traversal:} Inorder, preorder, postorder, level-order. Problems: validate BST, invert binary tree, maximum depth, serialize/deserialize tree.

    \item \textbf{Hash Map:} $O(1)$ lookup for counting, grouping, deduplication. Problems: two sum, group anagrams, LRU cache, subarray sum equals $k$.

    \item \textbf{Greedy:} Make the locally optimal choice at each step. Problems: jump game, activity selection, task scheduler, gas station.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Coding Interview Strategy}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Clarify the problem.} Restate it. Ask about edge cases: empty input? negative numbers? duplicates? Very large input?
    \item \textbf{Work through examples.} Trace through 2--3 examples by hand before coding.
    \item \textbf{Think aloud.} Explain your reasoning as you work. The interviewer is evaluating your thought process, not just the final answer.
    \item \textbf{Start with brute force.} State the simplest solution, analyse its complexity, then optimise.
    \item \textbf{State complexity.} For every solution, state time and space complexity using Big O.
    \item \textbf{Write clean code.} Use descriptive variable names. Break into helper functions. Handle edge cases.
    \item \textbf{Test your code.} Walk through your code with an example. Check edge cases (empty, single element, duplicates).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Example: Two Sum (LeetCode \#1)}]
# Problem: Given an array of integers and a target,
# return indices of two numbers that add up to the target.

# Brute force: O(n^2) time, O(1) space
def two_sum_brute(nums, target):
    for i in range(len(nums)):
        for j in range(i + 1, len(nums)):
            if nums[i] + nums[j] == target:
                return [i, j]
    return []

# Optimal: O(n) time, O(n) space using hash map
def two_sum(nums, target):
    seen = {}  # value -> index
    for i, num in enumerate(nums):
        complement = target - num
        if complement in seen:
            return [seen[complement], i]
        seen[num] = i
    return []

# Test
print(two_sum([2, 7, 11, 15], 9))  # [0, 1]
print(two_sum([3, 2, 4], 6))       # [1, 2]
\end{lstlisting}

\section{System Design Interview Template}

Use this template for every system design interview:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Requirements (5 min):} Ask clarifying questions. List functional and non-functional requirements. Agree on scope.
    \item \textbf{Estimation (3 min):} DAU, QPS (queries/second), storage, bandwidth.
    \item \textbf{High-level design (10 min):} Draw the architecture diagram. Show client, load balancer, app servers, database, cache.
    \item \textbf{API design (5 min):} Define key endpoints (REST or GraphQL). Show request/response formats.
    \item \textbf{Data model (5 min):} Define database schema. Discuss SQL vs.\,NoSQL choice.
    \item \textbf{Deep dive (10 min):} Zoom into the most critical or complex component.
    \item \textbf{Bottlenecks and solutions (5 min):} What breaks at 10x scale? How do you fix it?
\end{enumerate}

\section{Behavioural Interview Preparation}

\subsection{The STAR Method}

For every behavioural question, structure your answer as:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{S}ituation: Set the scene. What was happening?
    \item \textbf{T}ask: What was your responsibility or goal?
    \item \textbf{A}ction: What did you specifically do?
    \item \textbf{R}esult: What was the outcome? Quantify if possible.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Common Questions and Example Answers}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{``Tell me about a challenging technical problem you solved.''}

    \emph{STAR Example:} ``When building VeriRAG, I discovered that the LLM was generating hallucinated claims when source documents were ambiguous (\textbf{S}). I needed to build a verification layer to catch these (\textbf{T}). I designed a claim decomposition pipeline that broke responses into individual claims and cross-referenced each against the source documents using semantic similarity (\textbf{A}). This reduced hallucination rates by 40\% while maintaining response quality, as measured by our groundedness metric (\textbf{R}).''

    \item \textbf{``Describe a time you disagreed with a team member.''}

    \emph{Focus on:} How you communicated respectfully, sought to understand their perspective, and reached a resolution that was best for the project.

    \item \textbf{``Tell me about a time you failed.''}

    \emph{Focus on:} What you learned from the failure and how you applied that lesson going forward. Show growth, not perfection.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Portfolio Best Practices}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{GitHub profile:} Pin your best 4--6 projects. Each should have a professional README with: problem description, solution, tech stack, screenshots/demo, setup instructions.
    \item \textbf{Live demos:} Deploy projects where possible. A live demo is 10x more impressive than source code alone.
    \item \textbf{Clean commit history:} Meaningful commit messages (not ``fix'', ``update'', ``asdf''). No secrets in history.
    \item \textbf{Code quality:} Consistent formatting, docstrings, type hints, tests. Show you write production-quality code.
    \item \textbf{Personal website:} Showcase your best work, link to GitHub, include your CV/resume. Use a clean, modern design.
    \item \textbf{Blog posts:} Write 2--3 technical articles explaining something you built or learned. Demonstrates communication skills and deep understanding.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Coding interviews reward communication, structured thinking, and pattern recognition.
    \item System design interviews reward clarity, prioritization, and explicit trade-offs.
    \item Behavioural interviews reward reflection and ownership.
    \item Strong portfolios make your projects easy to understand, trust, and discuss.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What should you do before writing code in an interview?
    \item Why is the STAR structure effective in behavioural interviews?
    \item What makes a project portfolio persuasive to a recruiter or engineer?
    \item How do you turn a project into evidence of engineering judgement?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand interview preparation comprehensively: 12 LeetCode problem patterns, coding interview strategy, system design interview template, STAR method for behavioural interviews, and portfolio best practices. Practice consistently---aim for 150+ LeetCode problems before interviews.}

\chapter{Technology Comparison Reference}

This chapter provides side-by-side comparisons to help you choose the right tool for each job. Refer back to this when making architectural decisions.

\section{Language Comparison}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Python} & \textbf{JavaScript} & \textbf{TypeScript} \\
\midrule
Typing & Dynamic & Dynamic & Static \\
Execution & Interpreted & Interpreted/JIT & Compiled to JS \\
Primary use & Backend, ML, scripting & Frontend, full-stack & Frontend, full-stack \\
Speed & Moderate & Fast (V8) & Fast (compiles to JS) \\
Package manager & pip & npm / yarn & npm / yarn \\
OOP style & Class-based & Prototype-based & Class-based \\
Async & async/await & async/await, Promises & async/await, Promises \\
Best for & ML, data, APIs & Web, real-time & Large web apps \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{center}

Language & Python & Python & JavaScript \\
Philosophy & Batteries-included & Minimal & Minimal \\
ORM & Built-in & SQLAlchemy & Sequelize/Prisma \\

\section{Database Comparison}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{SQL (PostgreSQL)} & \textbf{NoSQL (MongoDB)} \\
\midrule
Data model & Tables with rows/columns & Documents (JSON-like) \\
Schema & Fixed, predefined & Flexible, dynamic \\
Relationships & Strong (foreign keys, joins) & Weak (embedded/referenced) \\
Queries & SQL language & MongoDB query language \\
Transactions & Full ACID support & Limited (improving) \\
Scaling & Vertical (+ read replicas) & Horizontal (sharding) \\
Best for & Structured data, complex queries & Unstructured, rapid iteration \\
Consistency & Strong by default & Eventual by default \\
When to use & Most applications & Logs, real-time, prototyping \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\section{Data Structure Comparison}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Structure} & \textbf{Access} & \textbf{Insert} & \textbf{Delete} & \textbf{Search} \\
\midrule
Array & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ & $O(n)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Linked List & $O(n)$ & $O(1)$ & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Hash Map & $O(1)$* & $O(1)$* & $O(1)$* & $O(1)$* \\
Binary Search Tree & $O(\log n)$ & $O(\log n)$ & $O(\log n)$ & $O(\log n)$ \\
Heap & $O(1)$ min/max & $O(\log n)$ & $O(\log n)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Stack & $O(1)$ top & $O(1)$ & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Queue & $O(1)$ front & $O(1)$ & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
\small{* Average case. Worst case for hash maps is $O(n)$ due to collisions.}

\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Session-based} & \textbf{JWT (Token-based)} \\

State & Stateful & Stateless \\

\end{tabular}

\section{Docker vs.\,Virtual Machines}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}

\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Docker Container} & \textbf{Virtual Machine} \\
\midrule
Isolation & Process-level & Hardware-level \\
Size & Megabytes & Gigabytes \\
Startup time & Seconds & Minutes \\
OS & Shares host kernel & Full guest OS \\
Performance & Near-native & Overhead from hypervisor \\
Use case & Microservices, CI/CD & Full OS isolation, legacy \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\section{REST vs.\,WebSockets vs.\,GraphQL}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{REST} & \textbf{WebSocket} & \textbf{GraphQL} \\
\midrule
Communication & Request-response & Bidirectional & Request-response \\
Connection & New per request & Persistent & New per request \\
Data fetching & Multiple endpoints & Push-based & Single endpoint \\
Over-fetching & Common & N/A & Never \\
Best for & CRUD APIs & Real-time (chat, live) & Complex data needs \\
Complexity & Low & Medium & Higher \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 37 — GLOSSARY
% ============================================================
\chapter{Glossary}

\begin{studybox}
Use the glossary for rapid revision and confidence checks. If a definition feels too abstract, return to the chapter where the term was taught with examples and context.
\end{studybox}

\begin{description}[leftmargin=2em, style=nextline]
    \item[Abstraction] Hiding complex implementation details behind a simple interface, exposing only what is necessary.
    \item[ACID] Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability---four guarantees for database transactions.
    \item[Activation Function] A mathematical function applied to a neuron's output in a neural network to introduce non-linearity (e.g., ReLU, Sigmoid, Softmax).
    \item[Algorithm] A step-by-step procedure or formula for solving a problem.
    \item[API] Application Programming Interface---a contract defining how software components communicate.
    \item[Asynchronous] Operations that do not block program execution while waiting for a result (e.g., network requests, file I/O).
    \item[Authentication] Verifying who a user is (``Are you who you claim to be?'').
    \item[Authorisation] Verifying what a user can access (``Are you allowed to do this?'').
    \item[Backpropagation] Algorithm for computing gradients in a neural network, enabling weight updates during training.
    \item[Batch Size] Number of training samples processed before model weights are updated.
    \item[BFS] Breadth-First Search---graph traversal using a queue, exploring level by level.
    \item[Big O Notation] Mathematical notation describing the upper bound of an algorithm's time or space complexity as input size grows.
    \item[Binary Search] Search algorithm that finds a target in a sorted array by repeatedly halving the search space. $O(\log n)$.
    \item[Blockchain] Distributed, immutable ledger technology used for secure, transparent record-keeping.
    \item[Cache] Fast storage layer that stores frequently accessed data to reduce load on slower systems.
    \item[CAP Theorem] In a distributed system, you can only guarantee two of three: Consistency, Availability, Partition tolerance.
    \item[CDN] Content Delivery Network---distributes static content to servers worldwide for faster delivery.
    \item[CI/CD] Continuous Integration / Continuous Delivery---automating testing, building, and deploying code.
    \item[Class] A blueprint for creating objects in object-oriented programming, defining attributes and methods.
    \item[CLI] Command-Line Interface---text-based interface for interacting with a computer.
    \item[Closure] A function that captures and retains access to variables from its enclosing scope.
    \item[CNN] Convolutional Neural Network---deep learning architecture specialised for image/spatial data.
    \item[Compiler] Software that translates entire source code into machine code before execution.
    \item[Concurrency] The ability to handle multiple tasks at the same time (not necessarily simultaneously---see parallelism).
    \item[Container] A lightweight, isolated environment for running applications (e.g., Docker container).
    \item[CORS] Cross-Origin Resource Sharing---browser security mechanism controlling which domains can access your API.
    \item[CRUD] Create, Read, Update, Delete---the four basic operations on data.
    \item[CSRF] Cross-Site Request Forgery---attack where a malicious site tricks a user into making unwanted requests.
    \item[Database Index] A data structure that speeds up data retrieval at the cost of additional storage and write overhead.
    \item[Decorator] A function that wraps another function to extend or modify its behaviour (Python pattern).
    \item[Deep Learning] Machine learning using neural networks with many layers to learn complex patterns from data.
    \item[Dependency Injection] Design pattern where dependencies are provided to a class rather than created inside it.
    \item[DFS] Depth-First Search---graph traversal using a stack or recursion, exploring as deep as possible before backtracking.
    \item[DNS] Domain Name System---translates human-readable domain names (google.com) to IP addresses.
    \item[Docker] Platform for building, shipping, and running applications in containers.
    \item[DOM] Document Object Model---tree representation of an HTML page that JavaScript can manipulate.
    \item[DRF] Django REST Framework---toolkit for building REST APIs in Django.
    \item[Embedding] A dense numerical vector representation of data (text, images) that captures semantic meaning.
    \item[Encapsulation] Bundling data and methods that operate on that data within a single unit (class), restricting direct access to internals.
    \item[Endpoint] A specific URL path in an API that accepts requests (e.g., \texttt{/api/users/}).
    \item[Epoch] One complete pass through the entire training dataset during model training.
    \item[ETL] Extract, Transform, Load---data pipeline pattern for moving and processing data between systems.
    \item[Feature Engineering] The process of creating, selecting, or transforming input variables to improve ML model performance.
    \item[Fine-tuning] Taking a pre-trained model and training it further on a specific dataset for a particular task.
    \item[Firewall] Network security system that monitors and controls incoming/outgoing traffic.
    \item[Framework] A pre-written code structure that provides a foundation for building applications (e.g., Django, React, Express).
    \item[Function] A reusable block of code that performs a specific task, accepts inputs, and returns outputs.
    \item[GAN] Generative Adversarial Network---two neural networks (generator and discriminator) competing to produce realistic fake data.
    \item[Git] Version control system for tracking changes in source code during development.
    \item[GPU] Graphics Processing Unit---specialised processor used for parallel computing, essential for deep learning training.
    \item[GraphQL] API query language that lets clients request exactly the data they need.
    \item[Hallucination] When an LLM generates plausible-sounding but factually incorrect or fabricated information.
    \item[Hash Map (Dictionary)] Data structure providing $O(1)$ average-case lookup by mapping keys to values.
    \item[Hashing] Converting input into a fixed-size string (hash) using a mathematical function. Used for passwords, data integrity.
    \item[HTTP] HyperText Transfer Protocol---the protocol browsers use to communicate with web servers.
    \item[Hyperparameter] A configuration value set before ML model training begins (e.g., learning rate, number of trees).
    \item[Idempotent] An operation that produces the same result regardless of how many times it is performed.
    \item[Inheritance] OOP mechanism where a child class inherits properties and methods from a parent class.
    \item[Interpreter] Software that executes source code line by line at runtime, without a separate compilation step.
    \item[JWT] JSON Web Token---compact, URL-safe token for stateless authentication. Contains encoded claims.
    \item[Kubernetes (K8s)] Container orchestration platform for automating deployment, scaling, and management of containerised applications.
    \item[Lambda Function] A small, anonymous function defined inline (also: AWS Lambda for serverless computing).
    \item[Latency] The time delay between a request and its response.
    \item[Library] A collection of pre-written code that you import and use in your own programs.
    \item[LLM] Large Language Model---AI model trained on vast text data capable of understanding and generating language (e.g., GPT, Claude, Gemini).
    \item[Load Balancer] Distributes incoming network traffic across multiple servers to ensure no single server is overwhelmed.
    \item[LSTM] Long Short-Term Memory---type of RNN with gates for learning long-range dependencies in sequential data.
    \item[Microservices] Architecture where an application is built as a collection of small, independent services.
    \item[Middleware] Software that sits between the request and the final handler, processing or modifying requests/responses.
    \item[ML] Machine Learning---algorithms that learn patterns from data to make predictions or decisions.
    \item[Mock] A simulated object used in testing to replace a real dependency (database, API, etc.).
    \item[Monolith] Architecture where the entire application is a single, unified codebase and deployment unit.
    \item[MVC] Model-View-Controller---architectural pattern separating data (Model), UI (View), and logic (Controller).
    \item[NER] Named Entity Recognition---NLP task of identifying and classifying entities (people, places, orgs) in text.
    \item[NLP] Natural Language Processing---AI for understanding, interpreting, and generating human language.
    \item[Normalisation] (Database) Organising data to reduce redundancy. (ML) Scaling features to a standard range.
    \item[OOP] Object-Oriented Programming---paradigm using classes and objects to model real-world entities.
    \item[ORM] Object-Relational Mapper---translates between database tables and programming language objects.
    \item[Overfitting] When a model performs well on training data but poorly on unseen data (memorised noise).
    \item[Parallelism] Executing multiple tasks simultaneously on multiple processors/cores.
    \item[Polymorphism] OOP concept where different classes respond to the same method call in different ways.
    \item[Prompt Engineering] The art of writing instructions (prompts) to make LLMs produce optimal output.
    \item[RAG] Retrieval-Augmented Generation---combining document retrieval with LLM generation for grounded answers.
    \item[Recursion] A function that calls itself to solve a problem by breaking it into smaller sub-problems.
    \item[Redis] In-memory data store used for caching, session management, rate limiting, and message brokering.
    \item[REST] Representational State Transfer---architectural style for designing APIs using HTTP methods and resource URLs.
    \item[RNN] Recurrent Neural Network---neural network with memory, designed for sequential data (text, time series).
    \item[Scalability] The ability of a system to handle increased load by adding resources.
    \item[SDK] Software Development Kit---collection of tools, libraries, and documentation for building on a platform.
    \item[Serialization] Converting data structures into a format suitable for storage or transmission (e.g., JSON, Protocol Buffers).
    \item[Sharding] Splitting a database into multiple smaller databases (shards), each holding a subset of the data.
    \item[SQL] Structured Query Language---language for querying and managing relational databases.
    \item[SSL/TLS] Secure Sockets Layer / Transport Layer Security---encryption protocols for secure internet communication.
    \item[Stack] LIFO (Last In, First Out) data structure. Also: technology stack (the set of tools/languages used).
    \item[State] Data that persists and changes over time in an application.
    \item[Stateless] A server/service that does not store client session data between requests.
    \item[TDD] Test-Driven Development---writing tests before writing the code they test.
    \item[TF-IDF] Term Frequency--Inverse Document Frequency---text vectorisation method weighing word importance.
    \item[Throughput] The number of operations or requests a system can handle per unit of time.
    \item[Token] (Auth) A string proving identity/authorisation. (NLP) A unit of text (word, subword, character).
    \item[Transformer] Neural network architecture using self-attention. Powers GPT, BERT, and all modern LLMs.
    \item[Transfer Learning] Using a model pre-trained on a large dataset and adapting it to a specific task.
    \item[Type Hinting] Annotating function parameters and return values with expected types for documentation and tooling.
    \item[Underfitting] When a model is too simple to capture the underlying patterns in data.
    \item[URL] Uniform Resource Locator---the address of a resource on the web.
    \item[UUID] Universally Unique Identifier---128-bit number used to identify resources without a central authority.
    \item[Validation] Checking that data meets required constraints before processing.
    \item[Vector Database] Specialised database for storing and searching high-dimensional vectors (embeddings).
    \item[Virtual Environment] Isolated Python environment with its own packages, preventing version conflicts.
    \item[Webhook] A callback mechanism where a server sends an HTTP request to a configured URL when an event occurs.
    \item[WebSocket] Protocol for full-duplex, bidirectional communication between client and server.
    \item[Word Embedding] Dense vector representation of words where similar words have similar vectors (Word2Vec, GloVe).
    \item[XSS] Cross-Site Scripting---attack injecting malicious scripts into web pages viewed by other users.
    \item[YAML] YAML Ain't Markup Language---human-readable data serialisation format used for configuration files.
\end{description}



% CHAPTER 38 — FINAL CAPSTONE CHALLENGES
% ============================================================
\chapter{Final Capstone Challenges}

These capstone projects test everything you have learned. Build at least two.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Full-Stack SaaS App:} Build a multi-tenant application with Django backend, React frontend, JWT auth, PostgreSQL, Docker deployment, and CI/CD. Include a subscription/payment system.

    \item \textbf{ML-Powered API:} Train a classification model, wrap it in a Flask API, containerise with Docker, deploy to AWS, add monitoring and logging.

    \item \textbf{Real-Time Chat System:} Build with Node.js/Express + WebSockets + React frontend + Redis for pub/sub + message persistence in PostgreSQL.

    \item \textbf{Data Pipeline + Dashboard:} Build an ETL pipeline that ingests data from multiple sources, transforms it with Pandas, stores in PostgreSQL, and visualises with a React dashboard or Power BI.

    \item \textbf{RAG System with Verification:} Build a research assistant that retrieves academic papers, generates summaries, and verifies claims with citation tracking. Evaluate with groundedness metrics.

    \item \textbf{Mobile-First Marketplace:} Build a React Native app with Django backend for buying/selling items. Include auth, search, filtering, image upload, and push notifications.
\end{enumerate}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 39 — NEXT STEPS TO BECOME ELITE
% ============================================================
\chapter{Next Steps to Become Elite}

\section{Continuous Learning}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Read code:} Study open-source projects on GitHub (Django, React, TensorFlow).
    \item \textbf{Build constantly:} Every month, ship something new or improve something existing.
    \item \textbf{Write:} Technical blog posts, documentation, architecture decisions.
    \item \textbf{Contribute to open source:} Start with documentation and bug fixes.
    \item \textbf{Stay current:} Follow Hacker News, dev.to, ArXiv (for AI papers).
    \item \textbf{Teach others:} Explaining concepts deepens your own understanding.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Advanced Topics to Explore Next}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{GraphQL:} Alternative to REST APIs.
    \item \textbf{WebSockets:} Real-time bidirectional communication.
    \item \textbf{Redis:} In-memory data store for caching and message brokering.
    \item \textbf{Celery:} Distributed task queue for background jobs.
    \item \textbf{Terraform:} Infrastructure as Code.
    \item \textbf{Monitoring:} Prometheus, Grafana, Sentry.
    \item \textbf{Microservices patterns:} Circuit breakers, service discovery, saga pattern.
    \item \textbf{Advanced ML:} Transformers, fine-tuning, RLHF, model deployment (TensorFlow Serving, ONNX).
    \item \textbf{Rust / Go:} High-performance systems programming.
\end{itemize}

\section{Final Words}

You now have a complete roadmap from zero to elite engineer. The knowledge in this handbook covers everything from how computers work to building production AI systems with verification. But knowledge alone is not enough---you must \textbf{build}, \textbf{practice}, \textbf{fail}, \textbf{debug}, and \textbf{ship}.

The engineers who become truly elite are the ones who never stop building. Start today. One project at a time. One chapter at a time. You have everything you need.

\begin{center}
\textbf{\Large Good luck, Amzat. Now go build something incredible.}
\end{center}

\end{document}
